==PAGE:1==
Development and Use of a Tool
Supporting Compositional
Veriﬁcation of Railway
Interlocking Systems
Anna Nam Anh Nguyen, s154230
Ole Benjamin Eilgaard, s154086
Kongens Lyngby 2020
==PAGE:2==
DTU Compute
Technical University of Denmark
Richard Petersens Plads, building 324,
2800 Kongens Lyngby, Denmark
Phone +45 4525 3031
compute@compute.dtu.dk
www.compute.dtu.dk
==PAGE:3==
Summary
The goal of this thesis is to develop and use a decomposition tool which supports
compositional veriﬁcation of railway interlocking systems. The veriﬁcation is
done by another tool developed by the RobustRailS project. The veriﬁcation
of large, complex railway networks requires many computational resources. To
address this issue, a compositional approach is used, utilizing diﬀerent types
of cuts and methods to decompose a large railway network. Given a railway
network layout and a cut through the network, the decomposition tool checks the
cut for wellformedness and decomposes the network accordingly into two sub-
networks. This way, decomposition is done automatically rather than manually.
To design the tool, the RAISE Speciﬁcation Language (RSL) is greatly used.
By creating and testing abstract speciﬁcations in RSL of the necessary functions
for the tool, the design and implementation processes become more streamlined.
The thesis builds on a command line interface (CLI) tool developed in another
thesis [Erd17]. The latter focuses on one certain type of cut whereas in this
thesis, a generalization of several cut types is made. Additionally, the decompo-
sition tool is able to project an interlocking table for a given network onto the
produced sub-networks, thus also producing sub-tables. Speciﬁcally, the end
product is a CLI tool which takes as input two XML ﬁles: one containing a net-
work and optionally its interlocking table, and one containing a cut. If certain
conditions hold, the tool produces two XML ﬁles, each containing a sub-network
and optionally its sub-table.
To assess how well the decomposition tool supports compositional veriﬁca-
tion, several experiments are done. The experiments compare the performance
of the veriﬁcation tool on monolithic networks and decomposed networks. Net-
works and interlocking tables of various sizes and complexities are used. The
performance of the veriﬁcation tool is shown to greatly increase when using
decomposed networks and interlocking tables produced by the decomposition
tool.
==PAGE:4==
ii
==PAGE:5==
Sammenfatning
Formålet med denne afhandling er at udvikle og bruge et dekomponeringsværk-
tøj (herefter "værktøjet"), som understøtter kompositionel veriﬁkation af jern-
banesikringsanlæg. Veriﬁkationen udføres af et andet værktøj, udviklet af Ro-
bustRailS projektet. Veriﬁkation af store, komplekse jernbanenetværk kræver
mange beregningsmæssige resurser. Derfor bruges en kompositionel fremgangsmåde,
der udnytter forskellige slags cut og metoder til at dekomponere et stort jern-
banenetværk. Givet et jernbanenetværk og et cut igennem netværket, tjekker
værktøjet velformetheden af cuttet og dekomponerer netværket til to sub-netværk.
Således udføres dekomponeringen automatisk frem for manuelt.
For at designe værktøjet benyttes RAISE Speciﬁcation Language (RSL) i
høj grad.
Ved at skabe og teste abstrakte speciﬁkationer i RSL af de nød-
vendige funktioner for værktøjet, bliver design- og implementationsprocesserne
mere strømlinet. Afhandlingen bygger på et kommandolinjegrænseﬂade (CLI)
værktøj udviklet i en anden afhandling [Erd17]. Sidstnævnte fokuserer på én
bestemt slags cut, hvorimod denne afhandling laver en generalisering af ﬂere
slags cut.
Derudover er værktøjet i stand til at projicere en rutetabel for
et netværk på de producerede sub-netværk og på denne måde producere sub-
tabeller. Slutproduktet er et CLI-værktøj, som tager to XML-ﬁler som input:
en med et netværk og eventuelt dets rutetabel, samt en med en cut speciﬁka-
tion. Hvis bestemte krav er overholdt, producerer værktøjet to XML-ﬁler, hver
indeholdende et sub-netværk og eventuelt dets sub-tabel.
For at evaluere hvor godt værktøjet understøtter kompositionel veriﬁka-
tion, udføres ﬂere eksperimenter. Eksperimenterne sammenligner præstationen
af veriﬁkationsværktøjet på monolitiske netværk og dekomponerede netværk.
Netværk og rutetabeller af varierende størrelse og kompleksitet benyttes. Præs-
tationen af veriﬁkationsværktøjet forbedres væsentligt, når der bruges dekom-
ponerede netværk og rutetabeller produceret af værktøjet.

Preface
This thesis was prepared at the department of Applied Mathematics and Com-
puter Science at the Technical University of Denmark under supervision of As-
sociate Professor Anne E. Haxthausen, in fulﬁllment of the requirements for
acquiring an M.Sc. in Computer Science and Engineering.
The goal of this thesis is to develop and use a tool which supports composi-
tional veriﬁcation of railway interlocking systems. The veriﬁcation is done by a
tool developed by the RobustRailS project.
The project period for the thesis was from January 21st 2020 to June 21st
2020.
Kongens Lyngby, June 21st 2020.
Ole Benjamin Eilgaard
Anna Nam Anh Nguyen

==PAGE:15==
Chapter 1
Introduction
The overall goal of this project is to provide a command line tool which can be
used for decomposition of large interlocking systems. In this chapter, we give a
motivation for the tool, explain the goal of the project and give an overview of
the structure of the report.
1.1
Motivation
It is highly important for railway systems to be safe. In order to prevent ac-
cidents such as collisions or derailments, interlocking systems are used. The
research project RobustRailS [Bru15], in which DTU is also involved, aims to
make rail transport more sustainable. Therefore, the project has developed a
veriﬁcation method and tool for automatic veriﬁcation of a speciﬁcation of Dan-
ish interlocking systems [VHP17]. The tool is compatible with the European
Train Control System (ETCS) Level 2 which moves away from physical signals
observed by a train driver to automatic, digital signals [Gro].
With an increase in the complexity of railway systems, more and more com-
putational resources are required for the veriﬁcation process. For this reason, a
compositional approach has been developed [MFH16] [FHM17]. The approach
uses diﬀerent kinds of cuts to decompose the speciﬁcation of an interlocking
system, such that veriﬁcation may be done on smaller parts of the monolithic
system. An existing tool for decomposition was developed by Cebrail Erdogan
in [Erd17]. However, the tool only supported one type of cut for decomposition.
==PAGE:16==
2
Introduction
1.2
Goal
The goal of this project is to develop a decomposition tool which accommodates
to several types of cuts. A cut speciﬁes where to decompose a given railway
network. The tool should support the existing veriﬁcation tool of RobustRailS
by producing smaller parts of the interlocking speciﬁcation which can be used as
input for veriﬁcation. During the development of the tool, we shall investigate
whether some parts of the existing decomposition tool can be reused. The tool
must also be experimented with to check whether the performance of the veri-
ﬁcation tool is increased when used on a decomposed interlocking speciﬁcation.
1.3
Structure
The report is structured such that it mostly follows the chronology of the project
work. The structure is therefore as follows.
Chapter 2: Background.
This chapter contains a description of relevant
background knowledge. This includes the concepts of the railway domain, the
existing veriﬁcation tool and the RAISE Speciﬁcation Language (RSL) used for
the later speciﬁcations.
Chapter 3: Analysis of Decomposition. This chapter contains an anal-
ysis of the diﬀerent types of cuts, decomposition methods and rules for applying
a generalized cut. This is includes the conditions which must be fulﬁlled for
decomposition. The analysis is necessary to deﬁne the requirements for the de-
composition tool.
Chapter 4:
Requirements for Decomposition.
This chapter describes
various types of requirements for the decomposition tool. This includes func-
tional, non-functional and RSL requirements.
Chapter 5:
Design of Decomposition.
This chapter presents the vari-
ous aspects of the design of the decomposition tool.
The design of the tool
covers the RSL speciﬁcation structure, the decomposition algorithm itself and
the structure for implementation.
Chapter 6: Implementation of Decomposition. This chapter discusses
the main implementation decisions. Additionally, the systematic testing is de-
scribed.
Chapter 7: Table Projection Feature. This chapter functions as both an
introduction to and analysis of the interlocking table projection feature which
extends the decomposition tool. The analysis covers the rules for projection and
==PAGE:17==
1.3 Structure
3
the conditions which must be fulﬁlled.
Chapter 8: Requirements for Projection. This chapter outlines the re-
quirements for the tool, now with the projection feature. That includes a re-
vision of the previously deﬁned requirements, along with a deﬁnition of the
requirements for the projection feature.
Chapter 9: Design of Projection. This chapter presents the design of the
tool, now with the projection feature. This includes a revision of the previous
design and a presentation of the projection algorithm.
Chapter 10: Implementation of Projection. This chapter discusses the
main implementation decisions for the projection feature. Also, sequence dia-
grams for the extended tool are explained. The systematic tests are also brieﬂy
explained.
Chapter 11: Experiments. This chapter describes the various experiments
performed using the existing veriﬁcation tool on both monolithic networks and
decomposed networks. The performance results of the veriﬁcation tool are pre-
sented.
Chapter 12: Discussion. This chapter discusses and evaluates the decom-
position tool, based on the deﬁned functional and non-functional requirements.
The limitations of the tool are also presented, as well as potential future work.
Chapter 13: Conclusion. This chapter concludes the thesis, summing up
the results of the work done.
==PAGE:18==
4
Introduction
==PAGE:19==
Chapter 2
Background
In this chapter, the relevant background knowledge is presented. Firstly, the
basic concepts of the railway domain are presented. Secondly, RobustRailS’s
existing veriﬁcation tool for interlocking systems is described. Thirdly, a brief
description of the RAISE Speciﬁcation Language (RSL) is provided.
The basic concepts are based on the new Danish route-based interlocking
systems. The information presented in the following sections, except for sec-
tion 2.4, is taken from our report "Formal Veriﬁcation Techniques and Tools for
the Railway Domain" [EN20]. The report was written during a special course,
as preliminary research to this thesis. The report functions as a summary of the
domain presented in [VHP17], so much of the text is similar to the referenced
articles. In the following sections, only small changes have been made to the
text from [EN20].
Since an interlocking system is responsible for the safety of a railway network,
it is a very important part of the domain. The system ensures that trains can
travel safely through the network. The concepts are therefore mainly concerned
with the speciﬁcation of an interlocking system. The system constantly monitors
track-side elements and changes their states appropriately to keep control of the
train traﬃc. The interlocking system is a part of the overall railway system.
The central components of an interlocking system are a railway network and an
interlocking table. In the following, these two components are elaborated.
==PAGE:20==
6
Background
2.1
Railway Networks
A railway network, interchangeably just called a network, comprises diﬀerent
types of track-side elements. The most relevant elements are linear sections,
points and marker boards. An example of the layout of a network, the Mini
network, is shown in Figure 2.1.
t10
t14
t13
t12
mb10
mb14
mb13
mb12
mb11
mb15
t20
mb21
mb20
t11
UP
DOWN
b10
b14
Figure 2.1: The Mini network layout, taken from [VHP17].
2.1.1
Linear Sections
A linear section is a section which has two ends: an up end and a down end.
Each end may have a neighbor which is also a section. For example, the Mini
network has the linear sections b10, t10, t12, t20, t14 and b14. Conventionally,
when a linear section is depicted as a horizontal line, the up end denotes moving
to the right, while the down end denotes moving to the left.
Linear sections are bidirectional by default, meaning that trains are allowed
to travel in both directions of a section, but not at the same time. A linear
section which only has one neighbor is also called a border section. The Mini
network has the border sections b10 and b14. Usually, they are depicted with
a dashed line. Border sections and their signals are typically controlled by the
external railway system.
A linear section which is neighbors with a border
section can also be called an exit section. The Mini network has exit sections
t10 and t14.
2.1.2
Points
A point is a section which has three ends: a stem, a plus and a minus end. Thus,
the section can have up to three neighbors. The Mini network has the points t11
and t13. A point can switch between being in a PLUS position and a MINUS
position. In the PLUS position, the stem is connected to the plus end, meaning
that traﬃc can ﬂow between the stem and the plus end. In the MINUS position,
the stem is connected to the minus end such that traﬃc can ﬂow between the
stem and the minus end. Thus, similar to linear sections, points are bidirectional
by default. However, traﬃc cannot travel directly between the plus and minus
ends.
==PAGE:21==
2.2 Interlocking Tables
7
2.1.3
Marker Boards
Along any linear section, up to two marker boards can be installed; one for
each direction. A marker board is associated with a virtual signal: OPEN or
CLOSED. The signal is sent to a train, signaling whether the train is allowed
to pass the speciﬁc marker board or not. For example, in the Mini network, t10
has one marker board mb11 in the down direction to signal trains which will
travel to b10. A marker board can only be seen by trains which travel in the
direction of which the marker board is installed.
In this thesis, we use the terms marker board and signal interchangeably. A
signal which resides on a border section in the direction of the section’s only
neighbor is called an entry signal. This is because it controls entrance to the
network. For example, the Mini network has entry signals mb10 and mb15.
Likewise, a signal which resides on an exit section in the direction of the border
section is called an exit signal. The Mini network has exit signals mb11 and
mb14.
2.2
Interlocking Tables
The second main component of interlocking systems is interlocking tables. The
new Danish interlocking systems are route-based, thereby the central part of
interlocking tables is routes. An interlocking table is always associated with a
railway network. The tables list the properties for routes and conditions for
using them. A route is made up of all the sections, between two deﬁned signals,
starting from the start signal to the end signal.
If there are no signals set
up in the same direction as the route and located between the start and end
signals, then the route is called an elementary route. An interlocking table is a
speciﬁcation of the elementary routes in the given railway network. Interlocking
tables are interchangeably called route tables. Each row in the table is a route
speciﬁcation which contains both properties and conditions for setting a speciﬁc
route. To set a speciﬁc route means to allocate resources, i.e. sections, point
positions and signals, and lock these for a route for a single train. Contrarily,
to release the allocated resources for a route is called releasing a route. Each
row in an interlocking table includes the following:
• id(r): the unique identiﬁer of route r
• src(r): the source signal of r
• dst(r): the destination signal of r
• path(r): the list of sections comprising r’s path from src(r) to dst(r)
• overlap(r): a list of the sections in r’s overlap, i.e. the buﬀer space after
dst(r) which is used in case trains overshoots the route’s path
==PAGE:22==
8
Background
• points(r): a map from points used by r to their required positions, i.e.
PLUS or MINUS
• signals(r): a set of protecting signals used for ﬂank or front protection for
the route
• conﬂicts(r): a set of conﬂicting routes which must not be set while r is set
Flank and front protection is the assurance that no train collides with an-
other from the side, via points, or from the front. A snippet of the interlocking
table for the Mini network is shown in Figure 2.2. The overlap column is omitted
since there are no overlaps for this network.
Figure 2.2: Snippet of the interlocking table for the Mini network, taken
from [VHP17].
2.3
The Existing Veriﬁcation Tool
In this section, the formal veriﬁcation tool is presented. As with the previous
section, the information presented in this section is gathered from [VHP17].
The given method for modelling and veriﬁcation of a railway interlocking sys-
tem combines domain-speciﬁc approaches and formal methods. RobustRailS
provides an environment for this, comprising the following components.
• A generic (conﬁgurable) behavior model, which depicts the Danish inter-
locking systems and its generic, conﬁgurable desired properties
• A domain-speciﬁc language (DSL) designed for the railway interlocking
domain
• A DSL speciﬁcation editor to construct the conﬁguration data of the in-
terlocking systems
• A static checker to check the static well-formedness of the conﬁguration
data
• A model generator which uses the generic model and a well-formed DSL
speciﬁcation to construct a concrete behavioral model of a given interlock-
ing system
• A properties generator which produces concrete properties. It takes as
input the description of generic properties and a well-formed DSL speciﬁ-
cation of conﬁguration data
==PAGE:23==
2.3 The Existing Veriﬁcation Tool
9
• A bounded model checker (BMC) which is able to perform k-induction 1.
2.3.1
Modelling and Veriﬁcation Process
The RobustRailS veriﬁcation method uses a toolchain for the modelling and
veriﬁcation process. An illustration of the toolchain is shown in Figure 2.3.
Figure 2.3: Veriﬁcation method and associated toolchain. This ﬁgure is taken
from Figure 3 of [VHP17].
There are four steps to the modelling and veriﬁcation process which utilizes
the aforementioned components. When using the given environment, ﬁrstly, the
conﬁguration data is speciﬁed via DSL. The data entails a railway network speci-
ﬁcation and an interlocking table speciﬁcation. Secondly, the data is checked for
well-formedness by the static checker. The static checker checks for any errors
in the network and interlocking table speciﬁcations. An example where the data
is invalid and the static checker would throw an error would be when a route
contains a certain destination but the section of the destination is not in the
route’s path. The static checker also detects if there are any conﬂicts which have
not been speciﬁed, but should have been. Thirdly, the generators instantiate
a generic behavioral model and generic safety properties with well-formed con-
ﬁguration data. Lastly, the BMC checks the generated model instance against
concrete properties. The check uses a combination of bounded model checking
and inductive reasoning. If the model instance does not satisfy the generated
properties, counterexamples are generated.
1More on k-induction in Section 2.3 of [VHP17].
==PAGE:24==
10
Background
2.3.2
XML Schema for an Interlocking System
The DSL of conﬁguration data is speciﬁed in XML format. Since this is the
input to both the decomposition tool and the veriﬁcation tool, it is important
to understand what the XML schema looks like. The DSL speciﬁcation can
be done manually or via a graphical user interface implemented by Andreas
Foldager [Fol15]. A Document Type Deﬁnition (DTD) ﬁle is also available for
the speciﬁcation. The ﬁle deﬁnes the syntax, i.e. structure and legal elements, of
the conﬁguration data in XML format. For the sake of easier understanding, the
syntax is elaborated and illustrated with an example in the following. Listing 2.1
shows an example of an XML representation of the Mini railway network.
The network element has an ID as attribute, e.g.
"mininetwork".
Note
that the network element is a child of the interlocking element which denotes
the interlocking speciﬁcation. The network contains track sections and marker
boards. A track section has attributes ID, length and type. The type denotes
whether it is a linear section or a point.
A track section can also contain
neighbour elements.
A neighbour element has two attributes: ref and side.
The former is a reference to another section. The latter denotes the end type of
the neighbour, i.e. up or down for linear sections, and stem, plus or minus for
points. A marker board has attributes ID, track, mounted and distance. The
track and mounted attributes denote respectively the ID of the track section
and the direction on which the board is placed. The distance attribute denotes
the distance from the marker board to the end of its track section.
Listing 2.1: Snippet of DSL speciﬁcation of an railway network with an empty
interlocking table partly taken from [VHP17]

An example of an XML representation of an interlocking table for the Mini
network is shown in Listing 2.2. Note that the table is represented as a route
table element and that Listing 2.2 can replace the empty route table element
in Listing 2.1.
A route table has attributes ID and network. For the interlocking system
of the Mini network, the route table has ID "miniroutetable" and network
"mininetwork".
The table can contain several routes.
A route element has
attributes ID, source and destination. Each route element may contain a num-
ber of conditions. A condition element represents a condition for setting the
route represented by the parent route element. A condition element consists of
the attributes ref and type. The value of type distinguishes between four diﬀer-
ent types of conditions: track vacancy, point, signal and mutual blocking. The
track vacancy type contains a reference to the sections which must be vacant,
i.e. the sections in the path or overlaps of a route. The point type contains a
reference to the point section whose position must be equal to the value of the
val attribute. The signal type contains a reference to a marker board which is
responsible for ﬂank and front protection. The mutual blocking type contains a
reference to conﬂicting routes. It is quite straightforward to see how the XML
representation reﬂects the table in Figure 2.2.

2.4
The RAISE Speciﬁcation Language
For the decomposition tool developed in this project, the RAISE Speciﬁcation
Language (RSL) is used to create and test the formal speciﬁcations before im-
plementing the tool in C++. RSL can be used to aid the design process where
speciﬁcations are made [Gro92]. The language contains features to create ab-
stract, formal speciﬁcations as well as concrete speciﬁcations which can be easily
translated to programming language. The language allows the user to specify
types, functions, modules and much more. The speciﬁcations may be executed
and tested in RSL via translation to SML. The RSL speciﬁcations created for
this thesis are introduced in chapter 5.
==PAGE:27==
Chapter 3
Analysis of Decomposition
In this chapter, an analysis of compositional veriﬁcation is made. The main focus
of the analysis is on the diﬀerent types of cuts which can be performed. The
analysis forms the basis of how to specify a general cut. Prior to the analysis,
an overview of how the decomposition tool shall support the veriﬁcation tool is
given. The chapter also touches upon diﬀerent methods for decomposition.
3.1
Supporting the Veriﬁcation Tool
Due to a large state space, veriﬁcation of large and complex railway systems
can be diﬃcult. The formal veriﬁcation method and tool of RobustRailS are
aﬀected by this problem due to limited computational resources. To address
this issue, a compositional approach has been developed [MFH16] [FHM17].
The compositional approach exploits the principle that the safety property of
an interlocking system is most likely not inﬂuenced by traﬃc on distant or
parallel routes or elements. Thus, the idea with the compositional approach is
to divide the railway network into regions which can be veriﬁed separately. This
approach reduces the state space when doing veriﬁcation.
The decomposition tool developed in this thesis is intended to support the
existing veriﬁcation tool described in section 2.3. The input of the veriﬁcation
tool is conﬁguration data, speciﬁed in the DSL which is XML. Therefore, the
decomposition tool should also output two interlocking speciﬁcations, containing
the sub-networks, in XML format. An illustration of how the tool supports
the veriﬁcation is shown in Figure 3.1. The input to the decomposition tool,
==PAGE:28==
14
Analysis of Decomposition
which is an interlocking speciﬁcation and a cut speciﬁcation, is also provided in
XML format. Note that the interlocking speciﬁcation needs only to contain the
network speciﬁcation and not the interlocking table speciﬁcation. A parser is
then used to convert between XML and the language chosen for implementation.
Verification Toolchain
Decomposition Tool
Interlocking 
Specification
Cut 
Specification
Decomposition 
tool
Interlocking 
Specification
Interlocking 
Specification
Figure 3.1: How the decomposition tool shall support the existing veriﬁcation
tool. The right-hand side of the ﬁgure is equivalent with Figure 2.3
which is from [VHP17].
3.2
Assumptions
The information in the remainder of this chapter is mainly gathered from [MFH16]
and [FHM17]. Since the compositional approach decomposes a network into
smaller chunks, some assumptions must be made on the divisions and trains in
order to properly verify the safety property.
1. There exist entry signals on the border sections to protect the entrances
to the railway network.
2. Routes in the network are elementary, as explained in section 2.2.
3. Trains can appear on a free border section at any time.
4. Trains on a border section can enter the network if the appropriate marker
board virtually signals OPEN.
5. Trains cannot enter in the middle of the network.
6. Trains that travel from a track element to a border section can disappear
at any time.
==PAGE:29==
3.3 Decomposing Networks via Cuts
15
These border assumptions, i.e.
assumptions on border sections, function
as an over-approximation of a railway network.
They are an abstraction of
a real environment. In a real environment there would be more restrictions,
such as minimum time intervals between two trains. The idea is that if the
safety property holds for the over-approximation, it should also hold in a real
environment.
3.3
Decomposing Networks via Cuts
Based on the border assumptions, a network controlled by one interlocking sys-
tem can be divided into multiple sub-networks, each controlled by its own inter-
locking system. Thereby, veriﬁcation of the overall interlocking system entails
verifying the interlocking systems of each sub-network. The division of the net-
work into multiple sub-networks is done via cuts. There exist diﬀerent types of
cuts and diﬀerent rules apply to each type. In this section, three speciﬁc types
of cut are presented: border cut, linear cut and horizontal cut. The information
for these types is obtained from [MFH16], [MFH17] and [FHM17]. Afterwards,
a generalization of the border cut and horizontal cut is made, represented as
the general cut.
3.3.1
Border Cut
The simplest type of cut is the border cut. When a border cut is performed on a
network, there are multiple conditions to adhere to. A decomposition which pro-
duces two sub-networks, a Down and an Up sub-network, by making one or more
border cuts must respect the following border cut conditions (BCCs) [MFH16]:
BCC-1. The cut separates two consecutive linear sections such that in the joint
between the two, there is an up marker board on the upper part of the
down section and a down marker board on the down part of the upper
section.
BCC-2. There are no overlap sections for one sub-network which include elements
in the other sub-network.
BCC-3. There is no ﬂank or front protection requirement on one sub-network which
depends on elements of the other sub-network.
These conditions ensure that the setting of any route depends only on the
elements belonging to one of the sub-networks.
Figure 3.2a shows an example of a network in which one can identify a
left-hand side sub-network and a right-hand side sub-network, i.e. the dashed
circles, connected by a joint between sections T2 and T3. A cut is performed
between the two sections, resulting in the two sub-networks "Down" and "Up",
see Figure 3.2b and Figure 3.2c. To abstract the opposing sub-network, T2 and
==PAGE:30==
16
Analysis of Decomposition
T3 are kept as the border sections of respectively the Down sub-network and
the Up sub-network. Recall that border sections are depicted as sections with
a dashed line instead of a fully drawn line.
T1

3.3.2
Linear Cut
Another simple cut is the linear cut which is presented in [MFH17]. In contrast
to the border cut, the linear cut is performed around a section. This does not
mean that the section is cut out from the rest. It means that because the section
functions as an interface, it is included in both sub-networks. The conditions
for the linear cut are similar to those of the border cut. An example of a linear
cut is shown in Figure 3.3. The interface is represented by the linear section T2.
Once cut, the interface is included in both sub-networks, and a border section
is added which abstracts the opposing sub-network. The border section also
includes an entry signal to satisfy the network assumptions of section 3.2.
The linear cut is not considered for the development of the decomposition
tool. The reason is that the linear cut is a very particular type of cut which may
not be used as often as other types of cuts. Having to consider and implement
both sorts would be more complicated.
==PAGE:31==
3.3 Decomposing Networks via Cuts

3.3.3
Horizontal Cut
The simple border cut disallows routes to pass through the cut, as shown in Fig-
ure 3.2; no elementary routes can go past exit sections and cross the cut. How-
ever, routes are often intertwined and diﬃcult to separate into independent
sub-networks. Therefore, this section describes a third type of cut which is the
horizontal cut. This type of cut allows routes to pass through the cut. The cut
is presented in [FHM17].
An example of a horizontal cut is shown on Figure 3.4. The horizontal cut is
a cut made between the minus ends of two points, t2 and t5 in the ﬁgure. The
resulting two sub-networks are conventionally named "High" and "Low", see
Figure 3.4b and 3.4c. Two consecutive linear sections are added respectively as
an exit section and a border section on the loose ends of the cut in order to
abstract the opposing sub-network. Entry and exit signals are also added, in
order to satisfy the network assumptions of section 3.2.
3.3.4
General Cut
In the previous sections, speciﬁc types of cuts are presented. By generalizing
these into a general cut, the user does not need to consider which type of cut
they are performing. Based on the border cut and the horizontal cut, a general
cut is deﬁned. The general cut will allow the user to cut on the following combi-
nations of sections: linear-linear, linear-point, point-linear and point-point. Any
combination of the sections’ ends is allowed, as well as any number of signals
on linear sections, i.e. zero, one or two. Also, for the general cut, we assume

BCC-2, cf. subsection 3.3.1. This means that there can be no overlap sections
for a route which appear in another sub-network when applying a general cut.
The condition is assumed as per supervisor Anne E. Haxthausen’s suggestion.
An elaboration of the conditions for general cut is found in section 3.5 where the
wellformedness and application of the general cut are explained. We also assume
BCC-3, i.e. the requirement of no front or ﬂank protection in one sub-network
depends on another sub-network. However, this condition is the most relevant
for the interlocking table projection feature which is introduced in chapter 7.
This is because ﬂank and front protection may be altered by this feature whereas
overlap sections may not be altered at all.
3.4
Decomposition Methods
We have now described three speciﬁc types of cuts and brieﬂy introduced the
general cut.
There also exist diﬀerent methods of using any type of cut to
decompose a network. This is called a decomposition method. In this section,
the diﬀerent decomposition methods are brieﬂy explained.
3.4.1
Single Cut
A single cut is the simplest form of decomposition method. The decomposition
is done via just one cut which divides the network into two sub-networks. This
method is mainly used for railway networks which are highly separated into
regions, connected by only a few sections. In Figure 3.2, assuming that the
Down and Up networks are only connected by T2 and T3, the ﬁgure illustrates
==PAGE:33==
3.5 Requirements for Decomposition via General Cut
19
where a single cut method is used to decompose a network.
3.4.2
Cluster Cut
A cluster cut is a collection of single cuts which are intended to be performed
simultaneously in order to produce two sub-networks. For more complex railway
networks, this is most likely the decomposition method to be used. Figure 3.5 il-
lustrates where a cluster cut must be used in order to produce two sub-networks.
mb1
mb2
mb4
mb3
t20
t12
t13
t21
t11
t10
Figure 3.5: An example of a network where a cluster cut must be used to
produce two sub-networks.
3.4.3
Multi Cut
A multi cut is a collection of single cuts or cluster cuts. The multi cut divides
the network into more than two sub-networks. This method is useful for very
large and complex networks which need to be divided into more than two sub-
networks in order to reduce the computational resources used by veriﬁcation.
To avoid complicating the requirement speciﬁcation by having to consider an
arbitrary number of sub-networks instead of just two, the multi cut method is
not considered for this thesis.
3.5
Requirements for Decomposition via General
Cut
Based on the various examples presented in regard to diﬀerent cut types and
decomposition methods, some requirements are established, both for the general
cut and for decomposition via the general cut.
3.5.1
Wellformedness of General Cut
In subsection 3.3.4, the general cut was described quite brieﬂy. In this section,
we present the actual conditions for wellformedness of a general cut for a given
==PAGE:34==
20
Analysis of Decomposition
network. For a general cut to be wellformed for a given network, the following
general cut conditions (GCCs) must hold:
GCC-1. A general cut is made between two sections.
GCC-2. The two sections are a part of the given network.
GCC-3. The two sections are distinct.
GCC-4. The two sections are neighbours. Note that this will imply GCC-2 and
GCC-3.
GCC-5. None of the two sections are a border section.
The reason for GCC-5 is that nothing is obtained from cutting at a border.
Also, exit signals are controlled by the external railway system.
Only entry
signals are controlled by the internal system. So, if a cut is performed at a
border section, a very small and trivial sub-network is produced, and the added
sections and signals only complicate the overall system. Note that the sections
used in a border cut are not necessarily border sections; the former just have
the appropriate signals for border cut.
3.5.2
Applying a General Cut
In this section, the diﬀerent rules of applying a general cut are presented. First,
the rules are explained overall. Then, the use of the rules is illustrated via dif-
ferent examples of applying a general cut.
When applying a general cut, either 1) sections and signals are copied from
one side of the cut to the other such that they exist in both sub-networks, or 2)
new sections and signals are added to a sub-network. The rules for the procedure
is as follows.
Given the two sections of a general cut, each of the sections will belong to
their own sub-network. If one of the sections is a linear section, the section
must be copied into the other sub-network as a border section where the cut
was performed. This also means that if both sections are linear, then each of
them will exist in the opposing sub-network as a border section. If none of the
sections is a linear section, i.e. they are both points, they will not be copied into
other sub-networks. Instead, if one of the sections is a point, then the other sub-
network needs a new linear border section to abstract the point. Additionally,
on the side of which a point is cut, two consecutive linear sections are added,
as previously illustrated with the horizontal cut. The exit section between the
point and the second section is always new and not a copy. The second section
is the border section, and as earlier mentioned, if the other section in the cut
is a linear section, it must be copied into the sub-network as a border section.
Otherwise, the border section is new.
==PAGE:35==
3.5 Requirements for Decomposition via General Cut
21
In regard to the signals, there must always be an entry signal and an exit
signal to a network, each residing on a linear section. Given the two sections
of a general cut, if both of them are points, no existing signals can be copied
into the other sub-network. Instead, new entry and exit signals are added to
both sub-networks. If one of the sections is a linear section, it may have zero,
one or two signals. A signal is only copied to the border section of an opposing
sub-network if it exists on the linear section in the direction of the opposing
network. So, if there are no signals on the linear section, a new entry signal is
added to the border section of the opposing sub-network, and a corresponding
new exit signal is added to the current section. If there are one or two signals on
the linear section, only the signal (if it exists) in the direction of the opposing
sub-network is copied into the opposing sub-network. If such a signal does not
exist, the procedure is the same as for no signals. If a point is involved in the
cut, it has no signals to be copied into the opposing sub-network. Then, the
procedure is the same as previously mentioned, except the new exit signal is
not placed on the point but on the new exit section between the point and the
border section.
To keep the existing marker board names was requested by supervisor Anne
E. Haxthausen, since it is used in a proof developed by her and a colleague,
regarding compositional veriﬁcation. Along the same lines, it is decided to keep
opposing linear section IDs. The alternative would be to create entirely fresh
sections and signals. If the new sections all had a suﬃx such as "border", then
it would be easier to identify borders. But, keeping the section names makes
it easier to see where two sub-networks connect. Even more importantly, when
creating interlocking tables, if a route goes from e.g. section t1 to section t3 in
the original network, but the two sections reside in diﬀerent sub-networks, the
route is still described as going from t1 to t3 in the interlocking tables for each
sub-network. So, if there is an overlap with another route at t3, keeping the
section ID t3 at the border prevents accidents if a train does not halt in time.
The ﬁrst main scenario is when the cut is applied between two linear sections,
shown in Figure 3.6. The section t1 has marker boards in both directions while
t2 has none. When cutting, since both sections are linear, they are added to
the opposing sub-network as a border section. So, both t1 and t2 exist in both
sub-networks. t1 has two marker boards, and mb2 is the one which points in
the direction of the Up network. Therefore, mb2 is copied as an entry signal
to the Up sub-network, see Figure 3.6c. In the Up sub-network, it acts as an
entry signal, whereas in the Down sub-network, it acts as an exit signal. If t1
only had the marker board mb2, the procedure would be the same. Since t2
has no marker boards, one must be added for each sub-network; mb_entry_t1
in the Down sub-network and mb_exit_t2 in the Up sub-network. As shown,
we introduce the naming convention that new signals take the IDs mb_entry_
or mb_exit_ followed a section ID. Speciﬁcally, mb_entry_ is used for entry
signals. The ID is followed by the ID of the neighboring section. mb_exit_ is
used for exit signals. Here, the marker board ID is followed by the ID of the
==PAGE:36==
22
Analysis of Decomposition

mb2
t1
t2
(a) The cut is performed between t1 and
t2
mb_entry_t1

The second main scenario is when the cut is applied between a linear section
and a point, shown in Figure 3.7. The following procedure is the same, regardless
of whether t2 is placed on the stem or the plus end of t1.
A point has no marker boards. Therefore, the section which is added to the
linear section t2 is given the ID and the signal t2_b and mb_entry_t2 respec-

t2. As with the ﬁrst main scenario, the appropriate marker board of the linear
section should also be copied. Since the linear section does not have a marker
board in the down direction, a new one is made with ID mb_entry_t1_ex to
act as an entry signal in the Down sub-network.
For the exit signal in the
Down sub-network, an exit section is added with ID and exit signal t1_ex and
mb_exit_t1_ex respectively. As shown, we introduce a naming convention for
exit and border sections added through a cut. If the new linear section is a
border section, the section ID is equivalent to the exit section followed by "_b".
This is shown in Figure 3.7c. If the exit section is between a point and a border
section, then its section ID is equivalent to the ID of the point followed by "_ex"
and the side which is cut, i.e. "_minus".

shown in Figure 3.8. The following procedure would be the same, regardless of
which end t1 and t2 connect at. For a cut between two points, a total of four

are points, all four new sections have fresh IDs and signals, upholding the nam-

new entry signals are situated on the border sections.
3.5.3
Preconditions for Decomposition
In order to decompose a network via the general cut, some preconditions must
be established. The wellformedness conditions of subsection 3.5.1 apply to a
general cut when using a single cut method on a given network. However, when
using a cluster cut method, we must deﬁne a few additional requirements for
the wellformedness of a cluster cut in the form of cluster cut conditions (CCCs):
CCC-1. A cluster cut is a set of wellformed general cuts.
CCC-2. All general cuts in a cluster cut are asymmetric. This means that the
same two sections cannot appear in two separate general cuts.
The reason for CCC-2 is that trying to perform a cut between the same two
sections twice is a waste of resources.
For simplicity, the cluster cut is the overall method considered in this the-
sis. The single cut method is then encompassed by the cluster cut in the case
where the cluster cut consists of only one general cut. The preconditions for de-
composition concern the input, namely the network and the cluster cut. These
decomposition input conditions (DICs) are deﬁned as follows:
DIC-1. The network is wellformed.
DIC-2. The cluster cut is wellformed.
DIC-3. The cluster cut separates the network into two regions.
DIC-4. No general cuts in a cluster cut are superﬂuous.
DIC-5. No general cut separates an overlap section from the last section of a
route’s path.
Initially, DIC-3 and DIC-4 were postconditions. But, to avoid running the
decomposition algorithm and only then ﬁnd out if the cut speciﬁcation pro-
duces two sub-networks without having performed any unnecessary cuts, it was
changed to a precondition. Also, DIC-5 is actually a rephrasing of BCC-2, cf.
subsection 3.3.1. This is because BCC-2 is worded as a post condition but if
we phrase it as a precondition like DIC-5, the condition is veriﬁed before de-
composing. Note that we assume that DIC-5 is only necessary to check if any
interlocking table is given for the network at all.
To further elaborate on DIC-3 and DIC-4, we present the following examples.
In Figure 3.9, a cluster cut is depicted which separates the network into three
==PAGE:39==
3.5 Requirements for Decomposition via General Cut
25
t1
t3
t5
t2
t4
t6
Figure 3.9: An example of a cluster cut which separates the network into three
regions, which breaks DIC-3.
t1
t3
t5
t2
t4
t6
Figure 3.10: An example of a cluster cut which in which one of the general
cuts are superﬂuous, which breaks DIC-4.
regions rather than two. This is not allowed for this thesis which is why DIC-3
is needed. In Figure 3.10, the general cut between t2 and t4 is superﬂuous; the
cut is not needed to produce two sub-networks. This is why DIC-4 is needed.
3.5.4
Post conditions for Decomposition
To ensure that the result of decomposition is correct, the following decomposi-
tion output conditions (DOCs) are deﬁned:
DOC-1. Border sections and exit sections are added appropriately to the sections
involved in a general cut.
Entry and exit signals must also be added
appropriately.
DOC-2. The IDs of the added elements of DOC-1 are fresh, i.e.
distinct from
existing IDs, with the exception of linear sections and relevant signals
involved in a cut.
DOC-3. The produced sub-networks are wellformed.
==PAGE:40==
26
Analysis of Decomposition
DOC-4. The networks do not have overlapping elements, except for the linear sec-
tions and relevant signals involved in a cut.
DOC-5. The union of the networks is equivalent to the original network with added
elements at the places where the original network was cut. To elaborate,
all elements, along with their relations, before decomposition must exist
in a sub-network afterwards. Any excess elements are the ones correctly
added for the cut.
DOC-1 and DOC-2 concern the correct application of a general cut as de-
scribed in subsection 3.5.2. DOC-4 is needed to ensure that the input network
has been divided into separate networks correctly. The reason that the linear
sections involved in a cut may exist in both sub-networks is that the exit signals
of a network are controlled like the corresponding entry signal on the comple-
menting network on the other side of the cut. DOC-5 ensures that no elements
in the original network have been lost from decomposition. All sections and sig-
nals, along with their relations, must be kept in one of the sub-networks. The
condition also ensures that no unnecessary elements have been added.
==PAGE:41==
Chapter 4
Requirements for
Decomposition
In this chapter, the diﬀerent requirements for the decomposition tool are pre-
sented. This includes functional requirements, non-functional requirements and
RSL speciﬁcations.
4.1
Functional Requirements
The functional requirements concern the actual functionality of the tool. We
deﬁne the following functional requirements (FRs):
FR-1. The tool can perform a given cluster cut of general cuts on a given network.
FR-2. The tool is implemented as a command line interface tool.
FR-3. The tool is able to parse networks and cluster cuts in XML format.
FR-4. The tool is able to write resulting sub-networks in XML format.
4.2
Non-Functional Requirements
The non-functional requirements concern the usability of the tool. We deﬁne
the following non-functional requirements (NFRs):
==PAGE:42==
28
Requirements for Decomposition
NFR-1. The tool must be compatible with the Linux OS system, like the existing
tool.
NFR-2. The XML schema for a cut should be formatted as simply as possible for
the user’s sake.
NFR-3. The tool should perform well no matter the size of the given network or
cut.
4.3
RSL Speciﬁcations
In chapter 3, the requirements for decomposition are formulated in spoken lan-
guage for ease of understanding. In this section, RSL is used to make formal,
concrete speciﬁcations of the requirements. The decomposition tool must be de-
signed to uphold these speciﬁcations. In that sense, deﬁning these speciﬁcations
supports FR-1.
4.3.1
RSL Modules
The RSL speciﬁcations can be divided into diﬀerent RSL modules. The structure
of the RSL modules is shown in Figure 4.1.
Interlocking
Decompose_REQ_DEF
Decompose_REQ
Decompose_AUX
Figure 4.1: The relation between the RSL modules for the requirements of the
decomposition tool. Interlocking is a module developed by Linh
H. Vu [Vu15]. The remaining modules are developed in this thesis.
==PAGE:43==
4.3 RSL Speciﬁcations
29
Interlocking is an existing RSL module developed by Linh H. Vu [Vu15].
In order to access existing functions and types which are needed to deﬁne the
RSL speciﬁcations, we use this module as an object. The module which uses
Interlocking is Decompose_AUX. This module contains the type deﬁnitions
for cluster cut and general cut as well as the value deﬁnitions of common and
auxiliary functions. Decompose_AUX is extended by Decompose_REQ_DEF.
The latter contains the value deﬁnitions of the functions which represent each
condition in subsection 3.5.3 and 3.5.4. Decompose_REQ_DEF is extended by
Decompose_REQ which is an implicit speciﬁcation of the decompose function.
It gives an overview of the requirements for decomposition.
One may notice that we use extensions rather than objects in RSL through-
out the thesis. The reason for this is that it is simpler when a module needs
to call functions from another module. If the former extends the latter, there
is no need to access an object representing the latter. For example, if Decom-
pose_REQ_DEF needs to access a function in Interlocking, either it would need
to have its own object for Interlocking or it would need to access an object for
Decompose_AUX and through that object access the object for Interlocking.
This is avoided by using extension. Though, from an object-oriented point of
view, it would be better practice to use objects and keep each of the modules
separated. But for simplicity, we refrain from doing so.
4.3.2
Types
First, an overview of the most relevant RSL types is presented. This includes
the types introduced for decomposition via general cut and the most relevant
existing types developed by Vu [Vu15].
For decomposition of a network, the most important existing type is the
NetworkLayout. A NetworkLayout represents a railway network and consists
of three maps of IDs to respectively linear sections, points and marker boards.
The type is deﬁned in a scheme, also named NetworkLayout, as the following
short record:
NetworkLayout ::
linears : SecId !
m Linear
points : SecId !
m Point
marker_boards : MbId !
m MarkerBoard
The NetworkLayout exists as an object in the RSL module Interlocking.
Linear sections, points and marker boards are also deﬁned as short records.
These comprise the information described in chapter 2 as follows:
Linear ::
neighbors : LinearEnd !
m SecId
length : Distance,
==PAGE:44==
30
Requirements for Decomposition
Point ::
neighbors : PointEnd !
m SecId
length : Distance,
MarkerBoard ::
section : SecId
dir : LinearEnd
distance : Distance,
Finally, the very basic types representing IDs, ends and lengths of sections
and marker boards are deﬁned as follows:
Id = Text,
SecId = Id,
MbId = Id,
Direction == UP | DOWN,
LinearEnd = Direction,
PointEnd == NB_STEM | NB_PLUS | NB_MINUS,
Distance = Nat
For the decomposition algorithm, we introduce the general cut and the clus-
ter cut as described in subsection 3.4.2 and section 3.5. A general cut simply
consists of a product of section IDs and a cluster cut is a set of general cuts.
The type deﬁnition resides in the module Decompose_AUX, as follows:
GeneralCut = SecId ⇥SecId,
ClusterCut = GeneralCut-set
We do not need an entire Section element to deﬁne a general cut, since the
existing types mainly use section IDs to access section information in maps.
4.3.3
Decomposition Requirements
As a starting point, the requirements for decomposition were listed abstractly
as preconditions and post conditions, cf. subsection 3.5.3 and subsection 3.5.4.
The input to the decomposition algorithm is a network and a cluster cut from
which the algorithm should output two sub-networks. Note for simplicity, we
do not use an interlocking type here as we only need to manipulate network
objects. The deﬁnition is found in Decompose_REQ, as follows:
decompose : I.L.NetworkLayout ⇥ClusterCut ⇥I.InterlockingTable
⇠
! I.L.
,! NetworkLayout ⇥I.L.NetworkLayout
decompose(n0, cc, rt0) as (n1,n2)
post post_decomposition(n0,n1,n2,cc)
pre I.L.is_wf(n0) ^ is_wf(cc,n0) ^ gives_two_subs(n0,cc) ^
,! no_overlap_outside(cc,rt0)
==PAGE:45==
4.3 RSL Speciﬁcations
31
Note that I is an object instance of the existing Interlocking scheme. This
enables access to the other scheme NetworkLayout as well as types and functions
already deﬁned in the two schemes. Furthermore, it makes it easy to diﬀerentiate
between the existing RSL speciﬁcations and those made during this thesis.
The value deﬁnitions of the post and preconditions are found in Decom-
pose_REQ_DEF. The wellformedness of a network (DIC-1) is checked via the
existing function is_wf. The wellformedness of a cluster cut (DIC-2) is deﬁned
in another function, also named is_wf for familiarity. gives_two_subs is a
function which checks that the cluster cut separates the network into two re-
gions (DIC-3) and none of the cuts are superﬂuous (DIC-4). The requirement of
not separating an overlap section from the last section of a route’s path (DIC-5)
is deﬁned in the function no_overlap_outside. The post conditions (DOC-1
to DOC-5) are collected in the function post_decomposition.
This function
consists of many sub-functions each of which speciﬁes a condition.
In the following sections, the RSL speciﬁcations for these preconditions and
post conditions are presented.
4.3.3.1
Preconditions
Since the speciﬁcation of wellformedness of a network (DIC-1), is already deﬁned
in the scheme NetworkLayout [Vu15], it is not explained in this thesis.
To
accommodate the wellformedness of a cluster cut (DIC-2), we must deﬁne CCC-
1 and CCC-2. The Cluster Cut type is already deﬁned as a set of General Cuts,
so the rest of DIC-2 is deﬁned as:
−−DIC−2. The cluster cut is wellformed.
is_wf : ClusterCut ⇥L.NetworkLayout ! Bool
is_wf(cc,n) ⌘(8scut : GeneralCut • scut 2 cc ) is_wf(scut, n)) ^
,! unique_cuts_in_cluster(cc),
We deﬁne the wellformedness of a general cut (CCC-1) by GCC-1 to GCC-
5. As mentioned in subsection 3.5.1, GCC-4 satisﬁes both GCC-2 and GCC-3.
GCC-1 is satisﬁed by the type of General Cut being a product of sections. So,
we deﬁne three sub-functions: one to check that both sections in a general cut
are part of the network (GCC-2), one to check that they are neighbors (GCC-4)
and one to check that neither section is a border (GCC-5).
−−The general cut is wellformed. Used for CCC−1.
is_wf : GeneralCut ⇥L.NetworkLayout ! Bool
is_wf((sid1,sid2),n) ⌘gc_part_of_n(sid1,sid2,n) ^ gc_not_border(sid1,sid2
,! ,n) ^ are_neighbors_irreﬂexive(sid1,sid2,n),
Each of the sub-functions are simply deﬁned as follows.
−−GCC−2. The two sections are a part of the same network.

Requirements for Decomposition

gc_part_of_n(sid1,sid2,n0) ⌘L.s_exists(sid1,n0) ^ L.s_exists(sid2,n0),
−−GCC−4. The two sections are neighbours.
are_neighbors_irreﬂexive : SecId ⇥SecId ⇥L.NetworkLayout

,! sid2,n0)),
Going back to DIC-2, what remains to be speciﬁed is the uniqueness of
general cuts in a cluster cut (CCC-2). A function for this condition is deﬁned
as:
−−CCC−2. All single cuts in a cluster cut are distinct . This means that the
,!
same two sections cannot appear in two separate general cuts.
unique_cuts_in_cluster : ClusterCut ! Bool

,! ) (sid2,sid1) /2 cc),
The next preconditions for decomposition are DIC-3 and DIC-4 which are
speciﬁed in the function gives_two_subs:
−−DIC−3. The cluster cut separates the network into two regions.
−−DIC−4. No general cuts in a cluster cut are superﬂuous.
gives_two_subs : L.NetworkLayout ⇥ClusterCut
⇠
! Bool
gives_two_subs(n0,cc) ⌘
let
−−Remove all neighbor relations in cluster cut
nDisconnected = remove_neighbors_in_cc(n0,cc),
region_set = get_regions(nDisconnected,L.sections(n0),{})
in
−−Check if traversal of networks gives two sub−networks
−−and no cuts are superﬂuous
card region_set = 2 ^ necessary_cuts(cc,region_set)
end
The sub-function remove_neighbors_in_cc removes the neighbor relations
between all sections which appear in the cluster cut from the given network.
The sub-function get_regions traverses the neighbor relations in a network and
ﬁnds the disconnected regions. Finally, the sub-function necessary_cuts checks
if any of the provided general cuts have been superﬂuous by seeing if the sections
==PAGE:47==
4.3 RSL Speciﬁcations
33
of a general cut are still reachable within the same region.
The speciﬁcations for each sub-function are shown and explained in the fol-
lowing. The speciﬁcations are also found in Decompose_AUX. First, there is
remove_neighbors_in_cc. The sub-function iterates through the general cuts
in the cluster cut.
For each general cut, the neighbor relation between the
two sections is removed, such that the resulting network contains all the same
sections, but without the neighbor relation between those which appear in the
cluster cut.
−−Disconnects the network at the cuts
remove_neighbors_in_cc : L.NetworkLayout ⇥ClusterCut
⇠
! L.
,! NetworkLayout
remove_neighbors_in_cc(n0,cc) ⌘
if cc = {} then
n0
else
let
(sid1,sid2) = hd cc,
ccTail = cc \ {(sid1,sid2)},
linears = L.linears(n0),
points = L.points(n0),
mbs = L.marker_boards(n0),
−−Remove neighbor relations
lNew = linears † [s 7! l_remove_nb(sid1,sid2,n0) | s : SecId • s
,! 2 linears ^ s = sid1] † [s 7! l_remove_nb(sid2,sid1,n0) | s : SecId
,!
• s 2 linears ^ s = sid2],

,! 2 points ^ s = sid1] † [s 7! p_remove_nb(sid2,sid1,n0) | s : SecId •
,! s 2 points ^ s = sid2]
in
−−Recursive call for the remaining single cuts
remove_neighbors_in_cc(L.mk_NetworkLayout(lNew, pNew,
,! mbs), ccTail)
end
end
Secondly, there is get_regions. The sub-function iterates through the set
of all section IDs of the given network.
During an iteration, the function
get_reachable is called to get the set of all reachable sections from the sec-
tion of the current iteration. This set of reachable sections corresponds to one
region. In the following iterations, only the sections which are not a part of this
region are explored in order to ﬁnd the remaining regions.
==PAGE:48==
34
Requirements for Decomposition
−−Find all regions in a disconnected network. Input is all sections in the
,! network.
get_regions : L.NetworkLayout ⇥SecId-set ⇥(SecId-set)-set
⇠
! (SecId-set)
,! -set
get_regions(n0, sidSet, networks) ⌘
if sidSet = {} then
networks
else
let
sid1 = hd sidSet,
nbSet = get_reachable(n0,{sid1},{}),
sidTail = sidSet \ nbSet
in
get_regions(n0, sidTail , networks [ {nbSet})
end
end
The sub-function get_reachable is speciﬁed as follows. It iterates through
the given set of sections tv. Any section in tv is added to the visited set of
sections v and their neighbours are added to tv such that all reachable sections
are found.
−−Find all reachable sections starting from one section
get_reachable : L.NetworkLayout ⇥SecId-set ⇥SecId-set
⇠
! SecId-set
get_reachable(n0,tv,v) ⌘
if (tv = {}) then
v
else
let
sid1 = hd tv,
tvTail1 = tv \ {sid1},
nb = L.get_neighbors(sid1,n0)
in
−−Prevent cycles
if sid1 /2 v then
let
tvTail2 = tvTail1 [ nb,
vNew = v [ {sid1}
in
get_reachable(n0,tvTail2,vNew)
end
else
get_reachable(n0,tvTail1,v)

end
end
end
Now, going back to the sub-functions of gives_two_subs, we have thirdly
the sub-function necessary_cuts. It is shown and explained in the following.
The speciﬁcation is also found in Decompose_AUX. Given the cluster cut and
the regions which the cut separates the network into, the sub-function iterates
through the regions and checks that for all general cuts, the two sections do not
exist in the same region.
−−Checks that all cuts are necessary, and not superﬂuous, to produce two
,! subnets.
necessary_cuts : ClusterCut ⇥(SecId-set)-set ! Bool

if regions = {} then
true
else
let
sidSet = hd regions,
rTail = regions \ {sidSet}
in
if (8(sid1,sid2) : GeneralCut • (sid1,sid2) 2 cc ) (8sidx :
,! SecId • sidx 2 sidSet ) (8sidy : SecId • sidy 2 sidSet ^ sidx 6= sidy
,! ) ⇠(sidx = sid1 ^ sidy = sid2)))) then
necessary_cuts(cc,rTail)
else
false
end
end
end
The ﬁnal precondition is DIC-5 which is deﬁned in the function no_overlap_outside.
−−DIC−5: No overlap section is included in a general cut.
no_overlap_outside : ClusterCut ⇥I.InterlockingTable ! Bool
no_overlap_outside(cc,rt0) ⌘
(8(sid1,sid2) : GeneralCut • (sid1,sid2) 2 cc ) (8r : I.Route • r 2
,! rng rt0 ^ len I.overlap(r) > 0 ) ⇠((I.path(r)(len I.path(r)) = sid1
,! ^ I.overlap(r)(1) = sid2) _ (I.path(r)(len I.path(r)) = sid2 ^ I.
,! overlap(r)(1) = sid1) _ (sid1 2 elems I.overlap(r) ^ sid2 2 elems I.
,! overlap(r)))))
The function simply checks that no general cut is made between: 1) the last
section in the path of a route and an overlap section, or 2) two overlap sections.
==PAGE:50==
36
Requirements for Decomposition
4.3.3.2
Post conditions
The function post_decomposition consists of various sub-functions to accommo-
date DOC-1 to DOC-5. As with the original network, the sub-networks must be
wellformed (DOC-3) which is checked via the existing function is_wf. So, the
remaining conditions need to be speciﬁed. In the following, post_decomposition
is speciﬁed and its sub-functions are explained. The sub-functions are speciﬁed
in Decompose_REQ_DEF, which can be found in appendix section A.1. .
−−The post conditions for output of decomposition.
post_decomposition : L.NetworkLayout ⇥L.NetworkLayout ⇥L.
,! NetworkLayout ⇥ClusterCut
⇠
! Bool
post_decomposition(n0,n1,n2,cc) ⌘L.is_wf(n1) ^ L.is_wf(n2) ^
,! no_overlap_in_networks(n1,n2,cc) ^ union_of_networks(n0,n1,n2,cc
,! ) ^ (8gc : GeneralCut • gc 2 cc ) added_border_elements(n0,n1,gc)
,! ^ added_border_elements(n0,n2,gc))
The sub-function no_overlap_in_networks checks that the two sub-networks
do not have overlapping elements, except for the linear sections and their as-
sociated signals which are involved in a cut (DOC-4). The correctness of these
elements are checked in the sub-function added_border_elements. This sub-
function applies the rules explained in subsection 3.5.2. It checks all the prop-
erties of an element which should be added by a cut (DOC-1 and DOC-2). The
sub-function union_of_networks ensures that the union of the sub-networks
is equivalent to the original network (DOC-5). The exception is the elements
which must be added by a cut. This means that the sub-function catches if any
unnecessary elements were added by decomposition. The speciﬁcation of each
sub-function is elaborated in the following.
First, we have no_overlap_in_networks which is speciﬁed as:
−−DOC−4. The networks do not have overlapping elements, except for the
,! linear sections which were part of cc.
no_overlap_in_networks : L.NetworkLayout ⇥L.NetworkLayout ⇥
,! ClusterCut ! Bool
no_overlap_in_networks(n1,n2,cc) ⌘(dom L.linears(n1) \ dom L.linears(n2
,! )) = dom get_set_of_l_in_cc(n1,n2,cc,[]) ^ dom L.points(n1) \
,! dom L.points(n2) = {} ^ (dom L.marker_boards(n1) \ dom L.
,! marker_boards(n2)) \ dom get_set_of_mb_at_cc(n1,n2,cc,[]) = {}
The sub-function checks that the intersection of linear sections in both sub-
networks is equal to the set of the linear sections which appear in the cluster
cut. The latter is determined via the sub-function get_set_of_l_in_cc. The
intersection of marker boards in the two sub-networks must also be empty,
with the exception of the marker boards which are copied from a linear section
to work as an entry signal on the border of an opposing sub-network. These
==PAGE:51==
4.3 RSL Speciﬁcations
37
marker boards are retrieved by the sub-function get_set_of_mb_at_cc. The
sub-function also retrieves the marker boards which are added, and not copied,
as a result of the cluster cut. This is why, unlike for the linear sections, the
intersection of the marker boards in the two sub-networks cannot be equal to
the result of the sub-function. Instead, the result of the sub-function is removed
from the intersection and the result of this should be the empty set. Lastly, no
points are copied when applying a cut, so these cannot overlap.
For the second sub-function, there is union_of_networks. The function is
speciﬁed as follows:
−−DOC−5. The union of the networks is equivalent to the original network
,! with added elements at the places where the original network was cut.
union_of_networks : L.NetworkLayout ⇥L.NetworkLayout ⇥L.
,! NetworkLayout ⇥ClusterCut
⇠
! Bool
union_of_networks(n0,n1,n2,cc) ⌘elements_exist_in_subnets(n0,n1,n2) ^
,! s_exist_in_net(n0,n1,cc) ^ s_exist_in_net(n0,n2,cc) ^
,! mb_exist_in_original(n0,n1,cc) ^ mb_exist_in_original(n0,n2,cc) ^
,! neighbors_exist_in_subnet(n0,n1,cc) ^ neighbors_exist_in_subnet(
,! n0,n2,cc)
The sub-function elements_exist_in_subnets checks that all linear sec-
tions in the original network exist in one of the sub-networks and if the section
has a marker board, it remains unchanged in the sub-network which the section
exists in. It also checks that all points in the original network exist in one of
the sub-networks.
The sub-function s_exist_in_net checks that all sections in a sub-network
exist in the original network, except those which have been added by a cut. The
sub-function mb_exist_in_original is a similar function for marker boards.
The sub-function neighbors_exist_in_subnet checks that the neighbor re-
lations in the original network are maintained in a sub-network, except for the
sections which were copied by a cut. If a section has been copied by a cut, it
exists in both sub-networks but its neighbour relations are diﬀerent. Therefore,
this section is excluded from the check.
The third sub-function is added_border_elements. The deﬁnition of the
function is quite long. It is found in appendix section A.1. The function receives
a general cut and checks if the necessary sections and marker boards have been
added to the sub-network according to both DOC-1 and DOC-2. It determines
which of the sections in the general cut resides in the sub-network, while making
sure to avoid choosing a section which has been copied rightfully. The function
then checks if this section has the correct new neighbors with their marker
boards which also should coincide with the naming conventions, fulﬁlling both
DOC-1 and DOC-2.
==PAGE:52==
38
Requirements for Decomposition
==PAGE:53==
Chapter 5
Design of Decomposition
In this chapter, the decomposition tool and algorithm are designed. Firstly,
the structure of the RSL modules is updated to accommodate the decompose
function. Then, the algorithm is ﬁrst presented informally, followed by a formal
RSL speciﬁcation. The technical aspects of the tool are addressed by a class
diagram and an XML schema for the cut speciﬁcation.
5.1
Additional RSL Modules
The structure of the RSL modules presented in subsection 4.3.1 must be up-
dated to accommodate the decomposition algorithm. The structure of the RSL
modules is shown in Figure 5.1. The yellow modules are the ones developed for
the decomposition algorithm. Decompose_EX contains the explicit deﬁnition of
the decompose function and its sub-functions. This is in contrast to the implicit
deﬁnition found in Decompose_REQ which is simply for overview.
Decom-
pose_EX extends Decompose_REQ_DEF in order to access common types
and functions as well as the preconditions for the decompose function.
The
decompose function is tested via the test modules Decompose_TEST, Three-
lines_TEST and Tiny_TEST which are elaborated in section 5.3.
5.2
The Decomposition Algorithm
There is no single solution to a decomposition algorithm. We have chosen one
approach but will also present an alternative which was considered. The main
==PAGE:54==
40
Design of Decomposition
Interlocking
Decompose_REQ_DEF
Decompose_REQ
Decompose_EX
Decompose_TEST
Threelines_TEST
Tiny_TEST
Decompose_AUX
Figure 5.1: The structure of the RSL modules for the explicitly deﬁned de-
compose algorithm. The Interlocking module was developed by
Linh H. Vu.
The remaining ones are developed in this thesis.
The green modules are mainly for decomposition requirements,
cf. subsection 4.3.1. The yellow modules are for the decomposi-
tion algorithm itself.
steps of the chosen decomposition algorithm are as follows.
1. Disconnect the network by iterating through all general cuts. This means
that for all general cuts in the cluster cut, remove the neighbor relation
between the two sections from the given network.
2. Get the two regions in the network by traversing it.
2.1. Take an arbitrary section.
2.2. Insert the selected section into one region.
2.3. Take the section’s neighbors.
2.4. For each neighbor, repeat step 2.2.
==PAGE:55==
5.2 The Decomposition Algorithm
41
2.5. Once all reachable sections have been found, take a new arbitrary
section which is not a part of the ﬁrst region.
2.6. Repeat step 2.2. where the sections are inserted into a second
region.
3. The ﬁrst sub-network equals the disconnected network where the ﬁrst
region is removed.
4. The second sub-network equals the disconnected network where the second
region is removed.
5. Lastly, add appropriate border/exit sections and entry/exit signals to each
sub-network, according to subsection 3.5.2.
Step 2.6 could be omitted, since we know from the precondition that there
are only two regions. However, as shown in the following, we want to utilize an
existing function get_regions to deﬁne the decompose function.
5.2.1
RSL Speciﬁcation of Decomposition Algorithm
To elaborate on the chosen decomposition algorithm, we explain the explicit RSL
speciﬁcation in the following. The value deﬁnition of the decompose function is
found in Decompose_EX, see appendix section A.2, as follows:
−−The decomposition algorithm
decompose : I.L.NetworkLayout ⇥ClusterCut ⇥I.InterlockingTable
⇠
! I.L.
,! NetworkLayout ⇥I.L.NetworkLayout
decompose(n0,cc, rt0) ⌘
let
−−Remove neighbor relations between sections of cc
nCut = remove_neighbors_in_cc(n0,cc),
−−Traverse network to get regions
regionSet = get_regions(nCut,I.L.sections(n0),{}),
region1 = hd regionSet,
region2 = hd (regionSet \ {region1}),
−−Remove region sections and marker boards from the disconnected
,! network
n1 = remove_sections(nCut,region1),
n2 = remove_sections(nCut,region2)
in
−−Add appropriate sections and signals
(add_borders_and_exits_at_cuts(n0,n1,cc),
,! add_borders_and_exits_at_cuts(n0,n2,cc))
==PAGE:56==
42
Design of Decomposition
end
pre I.L.is_wf(n0) ^ is_wf(cc,n0) ^ gives_two_subs(n0,cc) ^
,! no_overlap_outside(cc,rt0)
The precondition is the same as shown in the beginning of subsection 4.3.3.
The ﬁrst main step of the algorithm is to disconnect the network. This is done
via the sub-function remove_neighbors_in_cc. The sub-function is also used
in the precondition gives_two_subs where the network is disconnected to check
if the cluster cut separates the network into two regions. The sub-function is
shown and explained in subsubsection 4.3.3.1.
The second step is to retrieve the two regions of the disconnected network.
This is done via the sub-function get_regions which is also used in the precon-
dition gives_two_subs. The sub-function is shown and explained in subsubsec-
tion 4.3.3.1. The sub-function returns a set of regions and since the precondition
checks that there are only two regions, it is safe to extract the ﬁrst region as an
arbitrary region in the set and then the second region as the remainder.
The third and fourth steps are to set the sub-networks by removing one
region from the disconnected network. Note that if the original network was
used here instead, we would have to remove the neighbor relations once again.
The sections in the region are removed from the disconnected network via the
sub-function remove_sections. The sub-function simply updates the maps of
linear sections and points such that they do not contain those in the region. It
also updates the map of marker boards such that the marker boards placed on
a section in the region are removed. Otherwise, the marker boards would be
placed on a non-existing section.
The ﬁfth and last main step in the algorithm is to add appropriate sections
and marker boards to each sub-network.
This is done via the sub-function
add_borders_and_exits_at_cuts. The sub-function creates new sections and
signals or copies existing ones according to the rules of subsection 3.5.2. Note
that when adding a section, it is given the length 52 and its signal is given the
distance 51. This is because the required safety distance from a signal to the
tip of its section is at least 50 [Vu15].
5.2.2
Alternative Algorithm
An alternative to the algorithm is the following:
1. Duplicate the original network such that there are two identical networks:
the original network and the copied one.
2. Take an arbitrary general cut in the cluster cut.
3. In the original network, remove the neighbor relation between the sections
in the selected general cut.
==PAGE:57==
5.3 Testing the RSL modules
43
4. Choose one of the sections in the general cut and traverse its neighbors in
the original network.
4.1. Keep track of all the sections which have been explored.
4.2. If a section is met which resides in one of the general cuts in the
cluster cut, then remove the neighbor relation between the two sections in
the speciﬁc general cut. Once every reachable section has been explored,
then every general cut in the cluster cut has been checked.
5. In the original network, remove all the sections which have not been ex-
plored along with their marker boards.
6. In the copied network, remove all the sections which have been explored,
along with their marker boards.
7. Now the two networks should each consists of a half of the network.
8. At last, add appropriate elements according to subsection 3.5.2.
Duplicating the original network is not necessary; this was just the initial
idea for the alternative algorithm. One could simply set the sub-networks in the
same way of step three and four of the chosen algorithm.
The main diﬀerence between the chosen algorithm and this alternative is
when the network is disconnected. The chosen algorithm begins with discon-
necting the network. The disconnection is done by iterating through all general
cuts. In the alternative algorithm, traversal is used on the original network in
order to ﬁnd all the joints to disconnect. The chosen algorithm was selected
for implementation because of how the tasks are split up. First, the network
is disconnected.
Then, it is traversed to ﬁnd the regions.
This seems more
manageable to implement. Also, a function get_regions is already deﬁned to
ﬁnd regions as it is a part of the precondition. So, it can be reused for the
actual decomposition. With alternative, the traversal needs to include the task
of checking for a general cut and removing the neighbor relation. This may be
more tangled than the chosen algorithm.
5.3
Testing the RSL modules
Decompose_TEST contains tests for all important functions, including sub-
functions which is in appendix subsection C.1.1. It tests the decompose function
on the aforementioned Mini network in Figure 2.1. In the test, a cluster cut is
performed between a linear section and a point as shown in Figure 5.2. Three-
lines_TEST tests the decompose function on the Threelines network where the
cut is performed between two points, see Figure 5.3.
Tiny_TEST tests the
decompose function on a very small network consisting of four linear sections
where the cut is performed between two linear sections, see Figure 5.4.

5.4
Class Design
To implement the tool, it is helpful to draft the overall components ﬁrst. Based
on the RSL speciﬁcations, we deﬁne some main classes and their relations, shown
in Figure 5.5. The Decomposer class implements the decompose function. To
do this, it needs access to some of the auxiliary functions and types. The access
is achieved by inheritance from the class Decompose_AUX. This auxiliary class
uses the classes for cluster cut and interlocking. The Interlocking class contains
==PAGE:59==
5.4 Class Design
45
a reference to the class for networks, reﬂecting the existing RSL speciﬁcation.
Since a cluster cut consists of a set of general cuts, we also deﬁne a class
for general cut. Inheritance is used to follow the structure of the RSL modules
described in subsection 4.3.1. The inheritance-relation reﬂects the extension of
modules used in RSL. As an alternative, an object instance of Decompose_AUX
could be used in the Decomposer class. This would require that we keep track
of which functions belong to the Decompose_AUX class and which functions
belong to the Decomposer class. It seemed simpler to use inheritance which
would also follow the structure of the RSL modules.
The input and output are speciﬁed in XML format, so a parser must handle
the input and a writer must create an output ﬁle, cf. FR-3 and FR-4 of sec-
tion 4.1. Two parsers are used: one for the cluster cut speciﬁcation and one for
the interlocking speciﬁcation. One writer is used to output the two sub-networks
as interlocking speciﬁcations. In order to check the preconditions and post con-
ditions of the decompose function, we also deﬁne a requirements speciﬁcation
class ReqSpecDec. The class also needs access to auxiliary functions and types
which are inherited from Decompose_AUX.
