A more detailed class diagram is also created as shown in Figure 5.6. The
diagram shows the attributes and functions of each class. Note that the explicit
multiplicity of the relations is omitted since it is not the focus of the diagram.
The main function of the Decomposer class is the decompose function. Fur-
thermore, the sub-functions used exclusively by the decompose function are
listed as protected functions. The ReqSpecDec class contains all the functions
==PAGE:60==
46
Design of Decomposition
to check the preconditions and post conditions of the decompose function. The
purpose of the class is to implement the RSL module Decompose_REQ_DEF.
Likewise, the purpose of the Decompose_AUX class is to implement the RSL
module Decompose_AUX. The class contains all auxiliary functions. The only
diﬀerence between the RSL and this design is that the class does not contain
the type deﬁnitions for general cut and cluster cut. These are instead deﬁned
as separate classes. The classes for cluster cut and general cut are quite simple
as very few attributes and functions are needed. The only relevant functionality
to these classes is getters. To represent a network, we utilize the existing class
RttTgenDkIxlNetworkLayout via the interlocking class RttTgenDkIxlInterlock-
ing. Note that in Figure 5.6, the latter is simply called "Interlocking". The
Interlocking class contains references to the network class and relevant existing
types presented in subsection 4.3.2. This includes linear sections, points and
marker boards. When using this class it also makes sense to use the existing
parser and writer for networks. In Figure 5.6, the parser class is simply called
"InterlockingParser" and the writer class is simply called "InterlockingWriter".
A discussion of this decision is found in section 6.1.
5.5
XML Schema for a Cut
Since the input is given in XML format, and the schema is already deﬁned for
an interlocking, cf. subsection 2.3.2, we must deﬁne one for a cut. It is desirable
that the format is easy for the user to follow, cf. NFR-2 in section 4.2. The cho-
sen XML schema is based on the XML schema used in Erdogan’s thesis [Erd17].
The chosen schema is quite straightforward. An example of a proper input ﬁle
for the cut in Figure 5.3 is shown in Listing 5.1. A cut is represented by a
clusterCut element. The clusterCut element contains a number of general cuts,
represented by generalCut elements. A generalCut element contains two sec-
tions, represented by trackSection elements with an id attribute. As explained
in subsection 3.5.3, for this thesis, a single cut is considered as a cluster cut with
only one general cut. So, if the user wants to perform a single cut, they must
still use the clusterCut element but only provide one generalCut.
The chosen schema is a simpliﬁcation of the one in [Erd17] in the sense that
the user is not required to indicate which end of a section a cut is performed
on. For the schema in [Erd17], the user must state for a point section whether
the cut is on its stem, plus or minus end, and for a linear section whether the
cut is on its up or down end. Since the tool is intended to check if the stated
ends of sections in a cut are correct either way, we decide to relieve the user of
this task.
Listing 5.1: An example of the XML ﬁle for the cut in Figure 5.3.
<clusterCut id="threelinesClusterCut">
<generalCut id="threelinesGeneralCut1">

Chapter 6
Implementation of
Decomposition
In this chapter, the main implementation decisions are discussed. Furthermore,
the systematic testing is presented.
6.1
Parsers and Writer
In chapter 5 we mention the need of two parsers: one for the cluster cut and
one for the network.
Since the existing veriﬁcation tool already includes a
parser for a network via Libxml2 [Lib], we decided to reuse their parser class
RttTgenDkIxlConfigurationParser.
This parser class allows us to retrieve
the network layout.
For the cluster cut, we could try to acquaint ourselves
with Erdogan’s parsers for cuts and modify them to ﬁt with our tool. Since
we made some changes to the XML ﬁle format, we wanted to avoid potential
issues of missing entries. So, we could instead try to implement our own parsers.
It was easy to ﬁnd a way to implement a simple parser for the cluster cut via
RapidXML [Sch]. Therefore, we decided to implement our own parser for cluster
cut, while reusing the parsers for the network itself.
We reuse Erdogan’s XML writer to output the sub-networks. However, the
XML writer contains some functionality, such as writing a border cut, which
our tool does not need. Therefore, we exclude these functionalities.
==PAGE:64==
50
Implementation of Decomposition
6.2
Translating from RSL to C++
The tool is implemented by translating the RSL speciﬁcations of chapter 4 to
C++. Some parts of this are quite straightforward, such as declaring variables
and functions.
For other parts, especially the ones involving functions from
Vu’s RSL speciﬁcations, we had to make bigger adjustments. We also decided
to make some recursive functions iterative via loops. This came more naturally
to us. So, in RSL we are more comfortable with recursion, while in C++ we
prefer iteration. We only keep recursion for functions which need it, such as
get_point_direction where we traverse through a point’s neighbours.
In RSL, we use many comprehended value expressions. These are replaced
by loops and if-statements. Since we use vectors, maps and sets in C++, the
built-in functions make it much easier to alter the variables, in comparison to
RSL where union is used a lot. C++ also allows the use of references which
is why some of the return values and parameters of the functions in C++ do
not match the ones in the RSL speciﬁcations. For the sake of saving memory,
references are used many places instead of keeping the exact same return values
and parameters from RSL.
There were some issues with identifying the C++ functions corresponding
to the ones in Vu’s RSL speciﬁcations. In fact, some of the functions, which
our RSL speciﬁcation utilizes, could not be found.
So, we needed to create
some of these ourselves.
This includes getSections to retrieve all sections
from a network layout; in Vu’s C++ ﬁles, only a function to retrieve either all
linear sections or all point sections is found. Also, many of the existing C++
functions use the objects themselves as parameters, rather than their names.
This of course requires some extra implementation, as a direct translation from
RSL to C++ is not possible. The observing functions p_exists, l_exists and
s_exists are also not found. Some existing functions, such as findPoint and
findTrackSection can be used to assert whether they return a null pointer
or not, but it is more convenient to have a function which returns a boolean.
Therefore, we choose to make our own corresponding functions. We also use the
function is_boundary a lot, but it does not exist. Instead, we create our own
function which utilizes some of the other existing functions.
The preconditions and post conditions include wellformedness checks.
In
Vu’s RSL speciﬁcation, these wellformedness functions exist, but they are ac-
cessed via an Interlocking object which contains references to its network layout
and its interlocking table. The individual wellformedness functions are also pri-
vate, so we cannot call the functions directly in the C++ implementation. Ad-
ditionally, the functions do not return a boolean as otherwise speciﬁed in RSL.
For this reason, we unfortunately decide to omit the wellformedness checks from
the preconditions and post conditions of the decompose function.
==PAGE:65==
6.3 Additional Implementation Decisions
51
6.3
Additional Implementation Decisions
Ideally, the decomposition tool should have been implemented such that it was
completely detached of the remaining veriﬁcation toolchain. The veriﬁcation
toolchain contains many ﬁles and classes which are irrelevant to the decompo-
sition function. However, independence would require that we re-implement all
the required RSL types and functions such that they are free of dependencies.
We estimated that this would be too time-consuming and would rather spend
time on implementing the decomposition function itself. The downside to this
decision was that we spent much time familiarizing ourselves with the classes.
It also took much time to integrate the tool properly in the ﬁle system.
The preconditions, aside from those regarding wellformedness, of the de-
compose function are implemented as functions, corresponding to those in RSL.
The functions are called immediately when decompose is called. Thus, when
decompose is called, it immediately returns if the preconditions are not fulﬁlled.
We contemplated whether the post conditions should be implemented as tests
for the decompose function or as functions which were checked after calling the
decompose function. If we implemented the post conditions purely as tests, the
tests would become very large and chaotic. Therefore, we decided to implement
them as functions. This also meant that we had to test the post conditions
themselves, just as we test the preconditions.
The post conditions are then
called on the output of the decompose function. If the post conditions do not
hold, no output ﬁles are generated.
As Erdogan describes in Section 6.4 of his thesis [Erd17], networks cannot
be cloned properly via the default copy method for a network object. Since our
algorithm requires that we clone the monolithic network and remove elements
from it to produce a sub-network, we need a proper cloning method. When
using the default copy method, the old instance of the monolithic network and
the new instance of the monolithic network both refer to the same object. So,
both instances are altered when performing decomposition which is erroneous.
Therefore, we choose to reuse Erdogan’s cloning method with slight alterations.
The overall structure of directories and ﬁles is also reused from [Erd17]. The
structure is quite straightforward so there is no need to make any major changes
to it. For the same reason, the source code for the part of the command line
interface which shows the usage of the tool is reused with minor adjustments.
6.4

The C++ implementation of the functions must also be tested. We use the
open source test framework Catch2 [cat] to create and run unit tests.
The
framework is simply integrated as a single-header ﬁle and the syntax of writing
test cases is easily understandable. In [Erd17], Erdogan also used the previous
==PAGE:66==
52
Implementation of Decomposition
version of Catch2 which is Catch. We also looked into alternative unit testing
frameworks, such as CUTE [CUT] and Boost.Test [RE] but Catch2 seemed the
simplest to integrate.

We wanted to give proper access modiﬁers to functions of each class. This
way, only functions needed to be accessed by other classes were public. The
rest would be protected. Due to these modiﬁers, wrapper classes are used for
unit testing. Otherwise, the protected functions could not be tested. This also
meant that if a change was made to any function declaration of a class, it had
to be updated in the class’s header ﬁle, its .cpp-ﬁle and the test ﬁle if a wrapper
class was included for that particular class.
An example of a test case is shown in Listing 6.1.
This is the test case
for Figure 6.2. The test case has the name "Cut between a linear section and
a point" and the tag "[linearpoint]". Via the tag, the tester may run all test
cases which have the same tag. The test case contains two sections: one for
each cluster cut.
In each section, the preconditions and post conditions of
decomposition are checked via assertions, cf. the keyword REQUIRE.

==PAGE:71==
Chapter 7
Table Projection Feature
As an extension to the decomposition tool, the table projection feature is intro-
duced. The goal of the projection feature is to complement the decomposition
feature. Given an interlocking table belonging to a monolithic network, the goal
is to project the table onto the sub-networks. This results in two sub-tables,
i.e. an interlocking table for each sub-network. The feature allows the user to
specify custom routes and have them projected onto the decomposed network.
Normally, one would simply use the interlocking table generator provided by
the veriﬁcation tool to generate all the possible routes. But, in some cases one
may need to make manual adjustments to the routes. Without the projection
feature, one would have to repeat the action of creating these routes by hand
on the decomposed network.
In this chapter, the table projection feature is presented. First, it is shown
how the extended tool supports the veriﬁcation tool. Then, an analysis is made
to determine the rules for projection.
7.1
Extending the Decomposition Tool
In section 3.1, it is described how the decomposition tool supports the veriﬁca-
tion tool. Now that the decomposition tool is extended with a table projection
feature, there are some changes to how the tool supports the veriﬁcation tool.
In addition to a network and a cut speciﬁcation as input, an interlocking table
for the network can be provided. Together, the network and the interlocking
table form an interlocking speciﬁcation. But, an interlocking table can also be
==PAGE:72==
58
Table Projection Feature
empty which it is for the decomposition tool originally. The illustration in Fig-
ure 3.1 therefore remains unchanged. The diﬀerence is mostly internal. The
tool extracts the network and the interlocking table, if existing, from the in-
terlocking speciﬁcation, decomposes the network and projects the interlocking
table, if existing, onto the resulting sub-networks, producing two sub-tables.
The two interlocking speciﬁcations can then be used individually as input to
the veriﬁcation tool chain.
7.2
Analysis of Projection
The interlocking table projection feature centers around interlocking tables.
These are described in section 2.2. For the decomposition feature, a thorough
analysis was made to accommodate to diﬀerent cut types and decomposition
methods. For the projection feature, the analysis focuses on how to perform
projection. In particular, we must address how the placement of a cut relative
to a route in the monolithic network aﬀects the calculation of the projection of
the route.

The route r_3 shows a case where the route elements are all contained in the
sub-network of Figure 7.1b, but some elements have to be added to accommodate
a change in the sub-network in Table 7.3. Since the cut between t11 and t12
contains a point, the decomposition added two sections in Figure 7.1b. This
now means that the section t12 where mb12 resides is no longer neighbors with
t11. But for the original route r_3, the ﬁrst section in the path is t11. To
make up for this gap, the section t11_ex_plus which is between t11 and t12 is
added as r_3’s ﬁrst section in the path, and since this section contains a signal
mb_exit_t11_ex_plus which points in the opposite direction of the route, the
signal is also added to the route.
Lastly there is the r_1 route. Since the cut separates the source and des-
tination and path, the route has to be projected into sub-routes.
The ﬁrst
sub-route r_mb10_mb_exit_t11_ex_plus in Table 7.3 is created to accom-
modate the ﬁrst half of the route which is found in Figure 7.1b. The second
sub-route r_mb_entry_t12_mb13 in Table 7.2 contains the second half of r_1
in Figure 7.1c. The sub-routes have added signals and path sections, because
of the cuts and decomposition. An example of this is in
Figure 7.1b where
r_mb10_mb_exit_t11_ex_plus has an added path section at the end since
the cut added two sections after the point t11. The reason for mb12 to reside
within both of the sub-routes’ signals is because the decomposition copied sec-
tion t12 in Figure 7.1b, and this section retains its signal pointing towards the
rest of the sub-network to be used as an entry signal.
7.2.1
Rules for Projection
Depending on where a cut was performed, a route may contain elements which
appear in separate sub-networks. There are diﬀerent rules to this and several
may apply to the same route during projection. The rules are elaborated in the
==PAGE:74==
60
Table Projection Feature
following.
Rule 1: A cut was performed such that the route is fully contained in
one sub-network, i.e. its source, destination, path, protecting signals, protect-
ing points, overlap sections and conﬂicting routes all appear in the same sub-
network. In this case, no alterations are made to the route during projection.
The route remains the same in the sub-networks of which it is contained.
Rule 2: A cut involves only the section of the source of the route.
An
example of this is shown in Figure 7.2. The ﬁgure shows that the entire route
is still contained in one sub-network. This means that no alterations are made
to the route during projection.
mb2
mb1
t3
t4
t2
t1
Figure 7.2: A network with a route from mb1 to mb2. The cut only involves
the source of the route but does not aﬀect the route itself
Rule 3: A cut is made between the section of the source of the route and the
ﬁrst section in the path of a route. One example of this is shown in Figure 7.3.
Since the ﬁrst section in the path is linear, the source and its section are simply
copied as a border section. In this example, the route is still fully contained
in one sub-network. Another example is shown in Figure 7.4. Since the ﬁrst
section in the path is a point, an exit section is added between the section of
the source and the ﬁrst section in the path. This exit section must be added to
the path of the route.
Rule 4: A cut involves two sections in the path of a route. One example
of this is shown in Figure 7.5. The route is split in two as not all elements
are contained in the same sub-network. Another example for this rule is shown
in Figure 7.6.
Since one of the sections is the last section in the path, the
destination signal is moved to the other sub-network. The route is therefore
also split in two. In this case, the projection must result in two sub-routes.
The sub-routes must include the border/exit sections and their signals created
by decomposition. This means ﬁrstly that the paths need to include border
sections and exit sections, if any. Secondly, the protecting signals must include
the new entry signal for front protection. Thirdly, the destination of one route
must be updated to the new exit signal while the source of the other route must
be updated to the new entry signal.
==PAGE:75==

in Figure 7.7. In this case, the front protecting signals may have been altered.
This leads to the next rule.

Rule 6: A cut separates a protecting signal from the sub-network of the
remainder of the route. The same example as for the previous rule is used,
shown in Figure 7.7. We must update the protecting signals to only contain
signals within the sub-network. Additionally, in order to not lose protection, we
must replace the separated protecting signal with a signal created by the cut.
Rule 7: A cut separates a front protecting point from the sub-network of
the remainder of the route. An example is shown in Figure 7.8. Similar to
the previous rule, the protecting points must be updated to only contain points
within the sub-network. In order to not lose protection, we must also replace
the separated protecting point with a signal created by the cut.
Rule 8: A cut separates a ﬂank protecting point from the sub-network of the
remainder of the route. An example is shown in Figure 7.9. With decomposition
the point p4 is then no longer in the same network as the green route. Therefore
the green route must be updated with a new ﬂank protection.
Rule 9: A cut separates a conﬂicting route from the sub-network of the
remainder of the route. An example is shown in Figure 7.10. The example
shows that the green route will be decomposed into two sub-routes. The left
part of the green route no longer conﬂicts with the the blue route. The right
part of the green route still conﬂicts with the blue route, but since the green
route now has a new id the blue route must also update its conﬂicts to reﬂect

Rule 10: A cut separates an overlap section from the sub-network of the
remainder of the route. An example is shown in Figure 7.11. This case would
actually not be possible as long as the precondition DIC-5 holds, cf. subsec-
tion 3.5.3. Still, it is described in order to thoroughly cover the diﬀerent cases
==PAGE:78==

of cut placement.

Figure 7.11: A network with a route from mb1 to mb2 and an overlap which

Rule 11: A ﬁnal particular case which we consider is if a route is separated
into three or more parts by a cut. An example is shown in Figure 7.12. In the
example, two sub-routes should be created. The front-protecting signal mb3 is
in the same network as the ﬁrst sub-route as can be seen in Table 7.4 It should
not be included as a protecting signal in any of the sub-routes because their
protecting signals are updated through case 6. But, a user of this tool may
provide custom routes. It is therefore uncertain whether special circumstances
require a signal that seems unnecessary. We therefore decide to establish the
rule that all protecting signals for a route must be included in a sub-route as
long as they appear in the same sub-network.
Figure 7.13 shows another example of this along with Table 7.5. The ﬁrst and
third sub-route will share the protecting signals of their common sub-network.
If we were to avoid this, we could create an algorithm to only search for actual
protecting signals of a route. But, the user may intentionally have speciﬁed
some seemingly unnecessary signals which they want to keep.
One may wonder why we choose a diﬀerent approach for case 8 where all
conﬂicts of a sub-route must be in actual conﬂict with the sub-route when we do
not require that all protecting signals of a sub-route must be actual protecting
signals. This means that custom speciﬁed protecting signals can be preserved
after projection. The reason is that the process of preserving custom speciﬁed
conﬂicts after projection is much more complicated. For instance, if a conﬂict to
a route r is split into sub-routes, we must keep track of these sub-routes and out
of the sub-routes, we must select the ones, which are in the same sub-network
as r, as conﬂicts to r. If r was split into diﬀerent sub-routes, we would have
to repeat the process for all of its sub-routes. It is a much more complicated
process for conﬂicts in comparison to signals. Therefore, we choose to create
all sub-routes with an empty set of conﬂicts and then compute the conﬂicts as
one of the ﬁnal steps of projection. This may mean that we lose some custom
speciﬁed conﬂicts.
==PAGE:79==
7.2 Analysis of Projection
65

7.2.2
Naming Convention for Sub-routes
When routes are divided into multiple sub-routes, the sub-routes must be given
a new id.
For instance, if a route is divided into three diﬀerent sub-routes,
as shown in Figure 7.13, the two sub-routes in the same sub-network must
have diﬀerent ids. Therefore, we introduce the following naming convention. A
sub-route is given the id "r_src_dest" where src is the source and dest is the
destination. If multiple routes share source and destination but diﬀer in the
remaining elements, an underscore and a number are appended to their id. The
number ranges from 1 to the total number of routes which share the source and
destination. For example, if there are three sub-routes going from signal X to
signal Y and they diﬀer in their conﬂicts, they are given the ids "r_X_Y_1",
"r_X_Y_2" and "r_X_Y_3". It does not matter which id is given to which
sub-route.
Some sub-routes may also be come identical during projection. An example
of this is shown in Figure 7.14.
The identical sub-routes must not exist as
duplicates in the resulting interlocking table. They are simply represented by
the same route. The interlocking table is shown in Table 7.6.
7.2.3
Preconditions for Projection
In order to project an interlocking table of a network onto its sub-networks,
some preconditions are established.
Since projection is intended to happen
after decomposition, the main input for projection is a network, its interlocking
table and the produced sub-networks. The projection input conditions (PICs)
are deﬁned as follows:
PIC-1. The network is wellformed.
==PAGE:81==
7.2 Analysis of Projection
67
PIC-2. The interlocking table associated with the network is wellformed.
PIC-3. The sub-networks are wellformed.
PIC-4. The sub-networks are a correct decomposition of the original network, in
terms of the post conditions for the decomposition algorithm.
PIC-5. All sections and signals in the interlocking table exist in one of the sub-
networks.
PIC-4 may seem unnecessary since the tool is intended to perform projection
on the output of the decomposition which is also includes a post condition similar
to PIC-4. But, in case the projection feature is to be used separately from our
decomposition feature, it is nice to have PIC-4 as a precondition for projection.
Note that PIC-4 requires that a wellformed cluster cut is also provided as input
for projection.
7.2.4
Post conditions for Projection
To ensure that the projection is correct, some conditions must hold for the
generated sub-tables. So, we establish the following post conditions.
POC-1. The sub-tables are wellformed.
POC-2. The routes in the original table which have a source and destination within
the same sub-network remain the same in the associated sub table, with
the exception of appropriately updated protecting points and signals and
conﬂicts.
POC-3. The routes in the original table which have a source and destination in
diﬀerent sub-networks are split into several routes.
POC-4. All routes in a sub-table are either equivalent to or part of a route in the
original table.
BCC-3. No ﬂank or front protection requirement on one sub-network depends on
elements of the other sub-network.
For POC-2 and POC-3, the rules introduced in subsection 7.2.1 must be
applied. In subsection 3.3.4, we mention that BCC-2 and BCC-3 of subsec-
tion 3.3.1 also must hold for a general cut. BCC-2 was rephrased appropriately
as a precondition for decomposition, cf. subsection 3.5.3. BCC-3 however can
be included as it is as a post condition for projection.
==PAGE:82==
68
Table Projection Feature
==PAGE:83==
Chapter 8
Requirements for Projection
With the extension, we deﬁne additional requirements for the decomposition
tool. In this chapter, the diﬀerent types of requirements for the decomposi-
tion tool are updated. This includes the functional requirements and the RSL
speciﬁcations. The non-functional requirements remain the same, cf. section 4.2.
8.1
Functional Requirements
FR-1. The tool can perform a given cluster cut of general cuts on a given network.
FR-2. The tool is implemented as a command line interface tool.
FR-3. The tool is able to parse networks, with or without an interlocking table,
and cluster cuts in XML format.
FR-4. The tool is able to write resulting sub-networks with, if it exists, an inter-
locking table in XML format.
FR-5. The tool can project a given interlocking table onto the sub-networks of a
given network.
8.2
RSL Speciﬁcations
Based on the requirements for projection, RSL is used to make the formal,
concrete speciﬁcations of the requirements. The decomposition tool must be

extended to uphold these speciﬁcations. Deﬁning these speciﬁcations supports
newly deﬁned FR-5.

8.2.2
Types
For projection, we do not introduce any new types in contrast to decomposi-
tion where we introduce cluster cut and general cut. However, there are a few
relevant existing types, as speciﬁed in [Vu15], which we present in the following.
The purpose of the projection feature is to project an interlocking table of
a monolithic network onto its sub-networks. The feature is therefore centered
around interlocking tables and routes.
The type deﬁnitions for the two are
deﬁned in the abovementioned RSL module Interlocking. The existing type for
interlocking tables is InterlockingTable:
InterlockingTable = RouteId !
m Route
An interlocking table is thereby represented by a map from route IDs to
routes. Naturally, the existing type for routes is Route:
Route ::
source : MbId
dest : MbId
path : SecId⇤
overlap : SecId⇤
points : SecId !
m PointPos
signals : MbId-set
conﬂicts : RouteId-set
A route is thereby represented by a short record. It comprises the route’s
source signal, destination signal, sections in its path, overlap sections, protect-
ing points, protecting signals and conﬂicting routes. As with decomposition,
the deﬁnition of the basic types are quite straightforward.
The basic types
representing route id and required point positions are deﬁned as:
Id = Text
RouteId = Id
PointPos == PLUS | MINUS

Requirements for Projection

the two are presented.

The function covers the post conditions POC-2 to POC-5 and BCC-3. The
function checks that for all routes in the interlocking table, they either exist in
one of the sub-tables or they exist as sub-routes. The function get_subroutes
ﬁnds the correct sub-routes for a given route. The function routes_not_in_rt0
ﬁnds all the routes which are in the sub-tables but not in the original table. By
combining all the correct sub-routes, the function checks that the routes which
do not exist in the original table are all a part of the correct sub-routes.
One may wonder why we do not check that the naming convention is used
correctly in the post conditions for projection. For decomposition, we do check
the naming of sections and signals, cf. subsubsection 4.3.3.2. The diﬀerence is
that sections and signals are distinguished by their IDs. This is why we must
check the naming convention. For projection, routes are distinguished by their
properties, e.g. path, source, destination etc. The naming convention is not as
important.
Now, since get_subroutes is quite complex, we will elaborate it. The value
deﬁnition is found in Projection_AUX as follows:
−−Check the subroutes’ existence and correctness in the two route tables ,

dst = I.dest(orgRoute),

The function identiﬁes which sub-table and sub-network the ﬁrst part of
the original route is found in.
This is because the original route must have
been divided in at least two sub-routes. The ﬁrst part of the original route is
called the start route and it is found via the function get_start_route. Then,
the function identiﬁes the two sections in the path which were cut between.
This is done via the function get_cut_sections. The cut sections are used to
address any elements added by decomposition when the next sub-route is found
in the function get_subroutes_AUX. Finally, the found start route and the
remaining sub-routes are returned as the sub-routes for the given original route.
In the following, the sub-functions get_start_route, get_cut_sections and
get_subroutes_AUX are presented. The value deﬁnitions are all found in Pro-
jection_AUX. The deﬁnition of get_start_route is:
get_start_route : I.Route ⇥T.MbId ⇥T.SecId⇤⇥I.InterlockingTable ⇥I.L.
,! NetworkLayout ! I.Route
get_start_route(orgRoute, src, path, rtStart, nx) ⌘
let
index = if ⇠are_neighbors_irreﬂexive(section(I.L.marker_boards(nx
,! )(src)), I. ﬁrst (path)) then 1 else 0 end,
−−Map of the route with longest path in common with the given path
,!
(from the start of the path) and correct points and signals
mapWithSrc = [rid 7! rtStart(rid) | rid : T.RouteId • rid 2 rtStart ^
,! (8ridx : T.RouteId • (ridx 2 rtStart ^ ridx 6= rid ^ I.source(rtStart
,! (ridx)) = src) ) (common_path_length(I.path(rtStart(ridx)), path,
,! index,0) < common_path_length(I.path(rtStart(rid)), path,index,0))
,! ^ correct_points(rtStart(rid), orgRoute, nx) ^ correct_signals(rtStart
,! (rid), orgRoute, nx))],
startRoute = hd rng mapWithSrc
in
startRoute
end
The function ﬁnds the sub-route which has the longest path in common
with the original route.
The function common_path_length computes the
longest common path length between two paths given an index to start from
in each path. For the sub-path, an exit section may have been added at the
beginning, in case a cut has been performed at the start of the path. In that
case, the section of the source is not neighbors with the ﬁrst section in the
original path. This is why the index of the sub-path may be zero or one. For
==PAGE:90==
76
Requirements for Projection
the original path, the index represents where the sub-path starts in the original
path. This is simply zero for the ﬁrst sub-route. The source of the sub-route
must also be the same as that of the original route.
Otherwise, if the ﬁrst
section in the path was a point, the function might return a sub-route which
does not have the same source as the original route. The sub-route must also
have the correct protecting points and signals. This is checked by the functions
correct_points and correct_signals. correct_points simply checks that the
sub-route’s points are equivalent to all points which are in the original route
and the sub-network of the sub-route. correct_signals checks that the sub-
route’s signals are equivalent to the intersection of signals which are in the
original route and the sub-network of the sub-route. The intersection is used
here because additional signals may have been added due to a cut. For points,
additional points should not have been added due to a cut. One might wonder
why we do not use some of the existing functions which at ﬁrst glance seem
ﬁtting. An example of this is the existing function signals_exist_and_agree
which checks that When implementing the projection feature we decided to avoid
using some of the already created functions such as signals_exist_and_agree.
The function is not speciﬁc enough; we need to check not only that the signals
of the sub-route exist in the sub-network, but also that they exist in the original
route.
For the next sub-function get_cut_sections, the deﬁnition is:
−−Find the sections which were cut between. One section is in the subroute’
,! s path and both are in the original route’s path
get_cut_sections : I.Route ⇥I.Route ! T.SecId ⇥T.SecId
get_cut_sections(subRoute, orgRoute) ⌘
let
subPath = I.path(subRoute),
path = I.path(orgRoute),
subStartIndex = if I. ﬁrst (subRoute) 2 elems path then 0 else 1
,! end,
sidStartIndex = common_path_length(subPath, path,subStartIndex,
,! get_sub_start_index(subRoute, path) −1),
sidStart = path(sidStartIndex),
sidNext = if sidStartIndex > len path then "" else path(
,! sidStartIndex + 1) end −−in case there is no cut to look for
in
(sidStart , sidNext)
end,
The sections which were cut between must be one of the last elements in the
sub-path and both must be a part of the original path. To ﬁnd the index of
one of the sections in the cut, the function common_path_length is once again
used. For the original path, the index represents where the sub-path starts in
==PAGE:91==
8.2 RSL Speciﬁcations
77
the original path.
This is computed by the function get_sub_start_index.
Then, the common path length must be equal to the index of where the ﬁrst
section in the cut is. The second section is then found at the next index.
For the last sub-function of get_subroutes, get_subroutes_AUX, the def-
inition is:
−−Given a route from another subnet and routetable ﬁnds the following
,! subroute in the other subnet and subtable according to the original
,! route

,! ⇥I.InterlockingTable ⇥I.L.NetworkLayout ⇥I.L.NetworkLayout !
,! I.Route-set

src = I.source(r),
dst = I.dest(r),
path = I.path(r),
(sidBefore, sidNow) = routeCut,

foundDest = if I.dest(nextRoute) = dst then true else false end
in

{nextRoute}
else
let

,! rtNext,rtStart, nNext, nStart)

{nextRoute} [ routeSet
end
end
end,
The function is used to recursively ﬁnd the remaining sub-routes. The func-
tion is somewhat similar to get_subroutes.
Instead of ﬁnding a sub-route
which represents the start of the original route, a sub-route which represents
the middle or end part of the original route must be found. In this case, the
indices are diﬀerent. This is why the function get_next_route is used instead
of get_start_route. The function then checks if the destination of the original
route has been found in the sub-route. If not, a recursive call is made to ﬁnd
the remaining sub-routes. To show the diﬀerence between get_start_route and
get_next_route, we show the deﬁnition of the latter here:
==PAGE:92==
78
Requirements for Projection
−−Get the route which is the next of the subroute.
get_next_route : I.Route ⇥T.SecId⇤⇥T.SecId ⇥I.InterlockingTable ⇥I.L.
,! NetworkLayout ! I.Route
get_next_route(orgRoute, path, sidNext, rtNext, nx) ⌘
let
−−If the cut section is a point, we need to skip one index in the
,! path
index = if I.L.p_exists(sidNext,nx) then 1 else 0 end,
−−Map of the route with longest path in common with the given path
,!
(from the start of the path)
mapWithRoute = [rid 7! rtNext(rid) | rid : T.RouteId • rid 2 rtNext
,! ^ (8ridx : T.RouteId • (ridx 2 rtNext ^ ridx 6= rid ^ I.source(rtNext(
,! rid)) = I.source(rtNext(ridx))) ) common_path_length(I.path(
,! rtNext(ridx)), path,index,get_sub_start_index(rtNext(ridx),path)) <
,! common_path_length(I.path(rtNext(rid)), path,index,
,! get_sub_start_index(rtNext(rid),path))) ^ correct_points(rtNext(
,! rid), orgRoute, nx) ^ correct_signals(rtNext(rid), orgRoute, nx)],
nextRoute = hd (rng mapWithRoute)
in
nextRoute
end,
The similarity between get_start_route and get_next_route is that they
both ﬁnd the sub-route with the longest common path length. The diﬀerence is
the value of the index if a cut was performed at the beginning of the sub-route.
For get_start_route, the index is one if the source is separated from the ﬁrst
section in the original path. For get_next_route, the index is one only if the
ﬁrst section in the sub-path is a point. In that case, an exit section must have
been added to the path so the index must be one and not zero. If section instead
was a linear section, no section must have been added to the path. Furthermore,
since the sub-path starts in the middle of the original path, the function must
also determine the index to begin from in the original path. This is done via
the function get_sub_start_index.

Chapter 9
Design of Projection
In this chapter, the design of the tool is revisited and the projection algorithm
is presented. As with the decomposition algorithm, we ﬁrst restructure the RSL
modules to accommodate the project function. Then, we describe the projection
algorithm informally, followed by a presentation of the RSL speciﬁcations for it.
Afterwards, we revisit the class diagram of the tool.
9.1
Additional RSL Modules
The general structure of the RSL modules for projection bear resemblance to
the modules of the designed decomposition tool shown in Figure 5.1. In sub-
section 8.2.1, we added the modules for the requirements of projection. Now,
we update the structure to include the modules for performing and testing the
projection algorithm. The ﬁnal structure is shown in Figure 9.1.
As with the module Decompose_EX for the decomposition algorithm, the
module Projection_EX contains the explicit deﬁnition of the projection algo-
rithm and all the necessary sub-functions. Projection_EX extends the mod-
ule Project_REQ_DEF, mainly to access the preconditions of the projection
function.
The extension also allows access to the Interlocking module.
To
test the functionality and correctness of the projection algorithm, the module
Project_Test is created which extends Projection_EX.

9.2
The Projection Algorithm
The projection algorithm is as follows:
1. For both sub-tables, let the sub-table rti consist of all routes whose paths
are fully contained in the sub-network ni.
1.1 The route’s source and destination must exist in ni
1.2 If the ﬁrst section in the route’s path is a point, check if an exit
section must be added to the beginning of the path due to a cut
between the source and the path.
1.3 There may be protecting points and signals which were separated
from the sub-network due to a cut. So now, we add to the protecting
signals any appropriate ﬂank protecting signals.
==PAGE:95==
9.2 The Projection Algorithm
81
1.3.1 Add the protecting signals of the original route which exist in
the sub-network to the sub-route’s protecting signals.
1.3.2 For all points in the path, traverse out of its branches to check
that either 1) an existing protecting point is found, 2) an existing
protecting signal is found, 3) a natural border is met or 4) a new
border section added by a cut is met. In the latter case, the
appropriate protecting signal to add resides on the border.
1.3.3 The direction of ﬂank protecting signals to look for is always the
direction from the point to its stem neighbor.
1.4 Add to the protecting signals the front protecting signal which resides
on the section directly after the path.
1.5 Set the conﬂicts for the route as empty.
2. For each of the remaining routes, create the sub-routes.
2.1 First, create the sub-route which represents the start of the original
route and therefore has the same source as the original route.
2.1.1 The path of the sub-route is the longest sub-path of the original
path in the sub-network which contains the source.
2.1.2 If the last section in the sub-path is a border section, it must not
be added. This is because it is involved in a cut and therefore
exists in the other sub-network. The section should be included
in the next sub-route and not the current one.
2.1.3 If the second last section in the sub-path was a point, an exit
section must be added to the path.
This is because the exit
section does not exist in the original network but it does so in
the sub-network.
2.1.4 Do step 1.2
2.1.5 Set the destination of the sub-route as the signal of the last
section in the sub-path in the same direction of the source.
2.1.6 Set the protecting points as all points of the original route which
exist in the sub-network.
2.1.7 Set the protecting signals as all signals of the original route which
exist in the sub-network.
2.1.8 Do step 1.3-1.5.
2.1.9 Set the id of the sub-route as "r_src_dst" where src is the source
and dst is the found destination.
2.1.10 If the id of the sub-route and the sub-route itself already exists
in the sub-table, do not add it to the sub-table.
This means
that multiple sub-routes may share the same sub-route id, if the
sub-routes themselves diﬀer.
==PAGE:96==
82
Design of Projection
2.2 Secondly, create the next sub-route for the remaining path of the
original route on the other sub-network.
2.2.1 Do step 1.2.
2.2.2 The source of the sub-route is a signal whose section is boundary
and neighbors with the ﬁrst section in the remaining path. The
direction of the signal must be equal to the direction from its
section to the ﬁrst section in the remaining path.
2.2.3 Do steps 2.1.1-2.1.3 and steps 2.1.5-2.1.10
2.2.4 If the destination is equal to the destination of the original route,
all sub-routes have been created for the original route.
3. Rename any duplicate routes according to subsection 7.2.2 in order to
distinguish them from each other.
4. Add conﬂicts to all routes. For each route, a conﬂict must be physically
in conﬂict with the route.

add_conflicts represents step 4.

Testing the RSL modules for Projection
Projection_TEST found in appendix subsection C.1.2 contains tests for all im-

and its requirements on the very small network in Figure 5.4 with one route.
The route starts at e1 and ends at e4.
9.5
Class Design
For the class design, some additional classes are added to accommodate the
projection feature. A structure similar to the one for the decompose function is
used. The additional classes are Projection_AUX, ReqSpecPro and Projector
which are respectively representative of their RSL counterparts. The relations
between these classes are shown in the new domain model in Figure 9.2. In
order to access the necessary auxiliary functions and types, Projection_AUX
is a child of Decompose_AUX. The Projector class implements the projection
function. This class also needs access to auxiliary types and functions which
are inherited from the Projection_AUX class. The same goes for ReqSpecPro
which checks the preconditions and post conditions of the projection function.
Another change to the original domain model is that instead of a Network
class, an Interlocking class is used. As explained in section 7.1, the extended
decomposition tool must take as input an entire interlocking which comprises a
==PAGE:99==
9.5 Class Design
85
network and possibly an interlocking table. The parser and the writer for the
network is also renamed appropriately.

and their relations.
Furthermore, a detailed class diagram is shown in Figure 9.3. The diagram
contains an overview of relevant types and functions of each class deﬁned for
the tool. Note that the explicit multiplicity of the relations is omitted since it
is not the focus of the diagram.
The Projector class follows the same structure as the Decompose class. The
main function project and a setter for the requirement speciﬁcation class Re-
qSpecPro are the only two public functions of the class. The remaining functions
are protected because they are only used in conjunction with the project func-
tion. The classes Projection_AUX and ReqSpecPro are designed to resemble
their RSL counterparts as closely as possible. The Interlocking class contains
references to the classes for interlocking tables and networks. This is not shown
in order to limit the size of the class diagram. But, it is shown that an Inter-
locking has a network and an interlocking table as attributes. No particular
changes are made to the remaining classes originally created for decomposition.

Chapter 10
Implementation of
Projection
In this chapter, an overview of the implementation of the extended decomposi-
tion tool is presented in the form of sequence diagrams. Furthermore, the main
implementation decisions for the projection tool are discussed. In general, the
points discussed in section 6.2 also apply to the implementation of the projection
feature. Instead, this chapter contains the additional implementation decisions
made for the projection feature.
10.1
Sequence Diagrams
The reason that sequence diagrams were not shown in chapter 6 for the de-
composition tool was that we knew that the diagram would become much more
complicated once the projection feature was introduced. It might be confusing
for the reader to study a large sequence diagram only to re-study an even larger
revision of it later on. Therefore, we present the sequence diagrams now. One
may ask why we showed the diagrams in subsection 4.3.1 and chapter 9 which
were also revised later on. The diagrams were deemed quite simple straight-
forward. The changes to them in regard to the extension also seemed easy to
understand.
A sequence diagram for the main use of the tool is shown in Figure 10.1.
The user must provide an interlocking speciﬁcation and a cut speciﬁcation via
the options -i and -c. Then, the interlocking is parsed and its network layout is
retrieved. The cluster cut is parsed and retrieved, after which the requirement
==PAGE:102==
88
Implementation of Projection
speciﬁcation object is set for the Decomposer class. This gives the decompose
function access to the preconditions and auxiliary functions deﬁned in respec-
tively the ReqSpecDec and Decompose_AUX classes. Then, the interlocking
table is retrieved from the interlocking, and decomposition can be done. From
the main-function, it can be checked whether the preconditions held for the de-
compose function by checking if any sub-networks were returned at all. If the
sub-nets were returned, we move onto the next sequence diagram which depicts
what happens when the preconditions are true for decomposition. The sequence
diagram is shown in Figure 10.2.

Figure 10.1: Sequence diagram for the main use of the decomposition tool.
==PAGE:103==
10.2 Use of the Interlocking Parser and Writer
89
When the preconditions of decomposition hold, the main function checks
whether the post conditions also hold. If so, there are two ways to proceed. If
no interlocking table was provided, i.e. if the table contains no routes, the tool
simply writes the two sub-networks to separate XML-ﬁles. This is where the
sequence diagram would end for the decomposition tool without the projection
feature.
Now, if an interlocking table was provided, the project function is
called. The main function then checks if the preconditions held as well as the
post conditions. If so, the two sub-networks and their interlocking tables are
written to XML-ﬁles.
10.2
Use of the Interlocking Parser and Writer
In chapter 6 it was explained that the parser class RttTgenDkIxlConfigurationParser
is used to retrieve the network from the speciﬁed XML ﬁle. Helpfully, the class
also includes a function to retrieve the interlocking table. The writer class De-
compositionXmlWriter is also able to output an XML-ﬁle for a network with
its interlocking table, if the latter exists. We implement the tool such that if
an interlocking table is not given for a network, the tool is still able to decom-
pose the network and output two sub-networks without interlocking tables. The
tool simply avoids performing the projection. Thus, the functional requirements
FR-3 and FR-4 are both fulﬁlled, cf. section 8.1.
10.3
Translating from RSL to C++
One of the problems when translating the speciﬁcations for the projection fea-
ture from RSL to C++ was the mapping. For the projection feature, sub-routes
may share names, i.e. keys, while diﬀering in the remaining properties, i.e. val-
ues. A regular map type in C++ does not support one key having multiple
values. This was not an issue for the decomposition feature, so an RSL map
type was simply translated to a C++ map type. Fortunately, C++ has the mul-
timap type which supports multiple values per key. At the end of the projection
function, all the duplicate keys are renamed in order to convert the resulting
sub-tables to regular maps which are compatible with the writer.
The speciﬁcation of interlocking tables diﬀers in RSL and in C++. In RSL,
an interlocking table is deﬁned as a map consisting of route IDs and routes.
In C++, it is deﬁned as a class. Thus, one cannot use regular map operators
directly on the interlocking table. For this reason, throughout the source code,
after reading or creating an interlocking table variable, we immediately retrieve
the map from route IDs to routes and use the map as input and output to the
functions instead of the interlocking table object itself.
Furthermore, in RSL, the existing Interlocking module contains a func-
tion are_physically_in_conflict which is used to check the wellformedness
==PAGE:104==
90

of a route. We also utilize the function in RSL to generate the conﬂicts of a

==PAGE:105==
10.4 Additional Implementation Decisions

are_physically_in_conflict ourselves from RSL to C++ in a function with
the same name in the Projector class.
10.4
Additional Implementation Decisions
During the testing phase, we discovered an issue with the speciﬁcation of the
post condition checking that all routes exist in a sub-table either wholly or di-
vided. For example, given two routes which are nearly identical, only diﬀering
in the protecting points and signals, both are wholly contained in the same
sub-network. If a cut was performed which changes the protecting points and
signals of both routes, they may become identical. This means that their IDs
are diﬀerent, but the remaining properties are identical. We had two potential
solutions to this. Either, we could leave them as they are and have two duplicate
routes albeit with diﬀerent ids in the interlocking table. This does not break
the wellformedness of an interlocking table [Vu15] although it seems like super-
ﬂuous data. We could also choose to merge the two, as we do with sub-routes,
and apply the naming convention of subsection 7.2.2. We decided to keep the
duplicate routes but renamed them with a suﬃx at the end to keep to be able
to diﬀerentiate them and convert the multimaps to a normal map in C++.
10.5
Unit Tests
The main unit tests for the projection feature are made in the same manner as
the tests for the decomposition feature. In C++, we create test ﬁles to test all
important functions of the classes Projector, Projection_AUX and ReqSpecPro.
In section 6.4, the many diﬀerent combinations of sections for cuts were thor-
oughly tested for the decompose function. For the project function, we have not
made as extensive tests. We test the project function mainly on the previously
used Mini network shown in Figure 2.1, with its interlocking table. The ﬁle
containing the test cases is found in appendix subsection C.2.2. The cut used
to decompose the network is the same as previously shown in Figure 5.2. The
test results are shown in Table 10.1.

Chapter 11
Experiments
In this chapter, the experiments carried out on RobustRailS’s veriﬁcation tool
are presented. The experiments measure the performance of the tool in two
cases: when using a monolithic network and when using a decomposed network.
The latter comprises performing veriﬁcation on each sub-network. These ex-
periments are directly related to NFR-3, i.e. the tool should perform well no
matter the size of the network or cut.
11.1
Approach
When running the veriﬁcation tool, it is possible to retrieve performance results
which include the following values:
• The number of linear sections in the network.
• The number of point sections in the network.
• The number of signals in the network.
• The time it took to perform the veriﬁcation.
• The total size of memory used in MBs.
The most interesting values are time and memory usage. These will be the
center of the discussion to assess the overall performance of the veriﬁcation tool.
The discussion is found in chapter 12. In the following sections, the experiments
==PAGE:108==
94
Experiments
are described in terms of the input data, i.e. networks and interlocking tables
of varying size, and the results.
All experiments are run on a machine with an Intel Core i5 CPU 750 @
2.67GHz, 16 GB RAM, Ubuntu 14.04.1, Linux 3.19.0-80-generic x86 64 kernel.
Utilizing other speciﬁcations may result in diﬀerent results. The machine is a
server provided by RobustRailS. All necessary ﬁles for this project reside on the
server which we accessed remotely via ThinLinc. The reason for this is that a
connection to the DTU network was necessary to gain access.
11.2
Mini Network
The ﬁrst experiment is carried out on the simple network, Mini, previously
shown in Figure 2.1. The decomposition of the network uses the cut shown
in Figure 7.1.
The results of the veriﬁcation of the monolithic network and of its sub-
networks are shown in Table 11.1.

11.7
Results
The performance results show overall that the veriﬁcation tool performs much
better on a decomposed network. All the experiments show better performance
in terms of time and memory used.
==PAGE:112==
98
Experiments
The ﬁrst experiment was done on the Mini network in section 11.2. The
results show that the combined time used on veriﬁcation is less than the mono-
lithic network with 10.83 seconds for the sub-networks and 14.79 seconds for the
monolithic. The memory is also greatly reduced for the decomposed network.
The results show an improvement in performance but the network was quite
small to test on. Therefore, the following experiments use more complex and
larger networks.
It is especially interesting to see how much the results diﬀer between exper-
iments. A good example of this is the results from the Fismn network shown
in Table 11.3 and the Fismn network with fewer routes shown in Table 11.4.
The only diﬀerence between these two networks is the number of routes and this
has quite the eﬀect on the performance results. While the Fismn network with
the normal amount of routes went out of memory, the modiﬁed Fismn network
with fewer routes did not. The total time used when decomposing and doing
veriﬁcation on the sub-networks went from using 19146.8 seconds in the normal
Fismn network to using only 3729.74 seconds. So removing a little over half of
the original routes meant that it only took 19.5% of the time as it took with
the full amount of routes.
It is also worthy to note that the decomposed Fismn network with a large
number of routes did not cause the veriﬁcation tool to run out of memory. This
has been an important aspect of the tool, since this allows veriﬁcation to actually
ﬁnish within a reasonable time and memory usage.
The biggest improvement seen from the experiments which did not end with
out of memory for a network before decomposition can be found in the exper-
iment of the Point-Point network in section 11.3. The time spent went from
1321.6 seconds to 170.5 seconds. That is a decrease of 87% time spent. Not
only did the time decrease, but also the usage of memory. Memory usage went
from 2090.8 MB to 450.1 MB which is a decrease of 78.5%. This improvement
may also have something to do with the used cut.
After the cut, the total
number of routes are reduced to 24 from 46.
Most of the cuts used in the experiments are placed such that the sizes of
the resulting sub-networks are similar. However, this is not the case for the
experiment on the combined Fismn and Point-Point network in section 11.6.
Here, the cut is placed such that the decomposition results in two unbalanced
networks, where n1 is much smaller than n2 as shown in Table 11.5. Even though
this is the case, the total time spent and the memory used is still signiﬁcantly
lower than with the monolithic network.
==PAGE:113==
Chapter 12
Discussion
In this chapter, the decomposition tool is discussed and evaluated. The evalu-
ation is an assessment of how the tool fulﬁlls the functional and non-functional
requirements deﬁned in section 4.2 and section 8.1. This includes a description
of the tool’s limitations and potential work in the future.
12.1
Fulﬁllment of Functional Requirements
To assess how well the decomposition tool fulﬁlls the functional requirements,
we show the requirements again for convenience:
FR-1. The tool can perform a given cluster cut of general cuts on a given network.
FR-2. The tool is implemented as a command line interface tool.
FR-3. The tool is able to parse networks, with or without an interlocking table,
and cluster cuts in XML format.
FR-4. The tool is able to write resulting sub-networks with, if it exists, an inter-
locking table in XML format.
FR-5. The tool can project a given interlocking table onto the sub-networks of a
given network.
The decompose function fulﬁlls FR-1, as shown through systematic testing.
The tool is also implemented to fulﬁll FR-2. The use of the existing parser for
interlocking speciﬁcations and the created parser for cut speciﬁcations fulﬁlls
==PAGE:114==
100
Discussion
FR-3. The use of the existing writer in combination with the generated sub-
networks and sub-tables fulﬁlls FR-4. Finally, the project function fulﬁlls FR-5,
as shown through systematic testing.
12.2
Fulﬁllment of Non-Functional Requirements
To assess how well the decomposition tool fulﬁlls the non-functional require-
ments, we show the requirements again for convenience:
NFR-1. The tool must be compatible with the Linux OS system, like the existing
tool.
NFR-2. The XML schema for a cut should be formatted as simply as possible for
the user’s sake.
NFR-3. The tool should perform well no matter the size of the given network or
cut.
The experiments in chapter 11 show that NFR-1 is fulﬁlled since all the tests
ran on a Linux machine mentioned in section 11.1. NFR-1 should hold as long
as one has all the dependencies required as mentioned in the user guide of the
tool.
The XML format which was speciﬁed for cuts should partially uphold NFR-
2. An example is shown in Listing 5.1. The reasons for the format are described
in section 5.5. However, the format could be even simpler. It was chosen that a
clusterCut element should always be used even if just one generalCut element
was speciﬁed in the XML-ﬁle. It may confuse the user if they want to provide
a single cut and not a cluster cut. The reason that we designed the cluster cut
type to cover both cluster cuts and single cuts was that it seemed superﬂuous
to implement a single cut type. After all, a cluster cut can be deﬁned as a set of
general cuts, and a single cut can be represented as a cluster cut with just one
general cut. An alternative to implementing a single cut type is also to design
the parser to look for either a clusterCut element or a generalCut element as
the ﬁrst node. If a generalCut element is found, then a single cut must have
been provided by the user.
In regard to NFR-3, the biggest network that the decomposition tool was
used on was the combined Fismn and Point-Point network as shown in Fig-
ure 11.3.
The decomposition was done almost instantaneously, thus NFR-3
should be fulﬁlled at least up to the size of a network the same size of
Fig-
ure 11.3. Although not tested, we assume that the tool performs well with even
larger networks.
==PAGE:115==
12.3 Limitations of the Tool
101
12.3
Limitations of the Tool
Initially, when designing the projection feature, it was discussed whether it
should be its own executable, separate from the decomposition feature. Since a
precondition to the project function is that the input is a correct decomposition,
it was much easier to integrate the project function into the same executable.
The current way the tool is implemented does not allow the user to perform de-
composition without projection, unless the user provides a network without an
interlocking table. It is also not possible to perform projection without decom-
position, unless we implement some changes to the tool. These changes include
implementing an option to only perform projection and options to provide the
proper input for projection.
As described in section 6.2 it was not possible use the wellformedness check-
ing functions in the C++ implementation such that it complied with our RSL
speciﬁcations. Consequently, the tool does not check if the input is wellformed
which may lead to errors. The only wellformedness checks the tool can perform
are the ones for the cut speciﬁcation which we implemented ourselves. Alterna-
tively, the user can check if an interlocking speciﬁcation is wellformed by using
the veriﬁcation tool.
There are two other speciﬁc limitations to our tool, even when providing
a wellformed interlocking speciﬁcation and cut. The ﬁrst limitation is cutting
twice on the same linear section. The way we currently specify a cut, it can
still be wellformed as long as the two cuts only intersect at one section. Take
for example a cut c1 = (t1, t2) and c2 = (t2, t3). If t2 is a linear section, the
decomposition will fail in the post condition check. This happens because of
the way the tool names new sections at cuts. Since it is not considered which
end a linear is cut at, the border sections will end up with the same ID and
consequently overwrite each other.
The second limitation is when two linear sections become isolated in a sub-
network due to a cut, and the monolithic network contains at least one route
crossing both cuts. The decomposition will copy both of the two linear sections
as border sections at each end of the other sub-network. This means that one
sub-network actually contains all the same sections as the original network.
When projecting a route which should exist in both sub-networks, the route
will exist in only one of the sub-networks. This happens because of the way
the projection feature checks for routes that exist wholly within a sub-network.
These two limitations were left alone because it was reasoned that it would
not make sense to isolate one or two linear sections when decomposing for the
purpose of gaining better performance when using the veriﬁcation tool. Thus it
did not seem sensible to spend time on ﬁxing or implementing these restrictions.
==PAGE:116==
102
Discussion
12.4
Future work
The decomposition tool still has room for improvements. Everything mentioned
in section 12.3 could be candidates for things to ﬁx in the future.
Another
improvement is consistency.
In the source code, we should have been more
consistent in when to use the types vector, set, map, multimap etc, or when to
use two section IDs versus a general cut as parameters. A diﬀerence in coding
style can also be seen in the code between the decompose and the projection
features since we learned a lot from implementing the decompose feature, and
used some of the gained knowledge for the projection feature.
Some inconsistency is also found in the C++ tests. Instead of having one
way to write the tests, the structure changed a few times.
Some test were
written such that it grouped the public and protected functions into two test
cases, while other test were grouped according to a speciﬁc requirement being
tested. This could be more streamlined such that all the tests had a clear goal
and were structured using the same template. More test cases should also be
written, since presumably not all edge cases are tested yet.
==PAGE:117==
Chapter 13
Conclusion
The goal of the project was to provide a decomposition tool to support compo-
sitional veriﬁcation of large interlocking systems. In particular, a CLI tool has
been developed which given an interlocking speciﬁcation and a cut speciﬁcation
can decompose the former according to the latter. The cut speciﬁcation covers
both cluster cuts and single cuts which are comprised of a generalized cut type.
The tool was designed using the RAISE Speciﬁcation Language (RSL) to a
great extent. By executing and testing the RSL speciﬁcations, the transition
from design to implementation went somewhat smoothly. The tool was depen-
dent on an existing veriﬁcation tool developed by RobustRailS, and because
there were some inconsistencies between the RSL speciﬁcation and the imple-
mentation of the veriﬁcation tool, some workarounds needed to be made. The
consequence was that the implementation of the decomposition tool had some
noticeable diﬀerences from its RSL speciﬁcations.
Overall, the requirements of the tool which concern both the functional and
non-functional aspects have been fulﬁlled to a satisfying degree. Through sys-
tematic testing, the functional requirements are shown to hold. In short, the
tool is able to both decompose a network and project its interlocking table onto
the produced sub-networks. Regarding the non-functional requirements, ﬁrstly
the tool has been implemented to be compatible with the Linux OS system
because the existing veriﬁcation tool is. Secondly, a quite simple XML schema
for a cut speciﬁcation has been deﬁned. But, there is still a little room for im-
provement in regard to user-friendliness. Thirdly, the tool is shown to perform
decomposition on networks of varying sizes almost instantaneously. It does not
mean that the tool performs well no matter the size, as one can create even
==PAGE:118==
104
Conclusion
larger, more complex networks than the ones used in this thesis.
It was also interesting to see how well the tool supports compositional veriﬁ-
cation. For that reason, several experiments were performed. The experiments
entailed comparing the performance of the veriﬁcation tool when using mono-
lithic networks and decomposed networks. The results show that the veriﬁcation
tool performs much better when used on decomposed networks than on mono-
lithic networks.

I.L.signals (sid_border, nx)(T.DOWN) = "mb_entry_" b
,! sid
end
else
T.DOWN 2 I.L.signals(sid, nx) ^ T.UP 2 I.L.signals(sid_border,
,! nx) ^
if I.L.l_exists(sid_other, n0) then
(sid_other = sid_border ^
if T.UP 2 I.L.signals(sid_other, n0) then
I.L.signals (sid_other, n0)(T.UP) = I.L.signals(
,! sid_border, nx)(T.UP)
else
I.L.signals (sid_border, nx)(T.UP) = "mb_entry_" b
,! sid
end)
else
I.L.signals (sid_border, nx)(T.UP) = "mb_entry_" b sid
end
end
end
end
end,
−−DOC−4. The networks do not have overlapping elements, except for the linear
,! sections which were part of cc.

,! get_set_of_mb_at_cc(n1,n2,cc,{}) = {},

−−The decomposition algorithm
decompose : I.L.NetworkLayout ⇥ClusterCut ⇥I.InterlockingTable ⇠
! I.L.
,! NetworkLayout ⇥I.L.NetworkLayout
decompose(n0,cc, rt0) ⌘

,! network

n2 = remove_sections(nCut,region2)

−−Add appropriate sections and signals
(add_borders_and_exits_at_cuts(n0,n1,cc),
,! add_borders_and_exits_at_cuts(n0,n2,cc))

−−Change the ids of all routes which share the same id
rename_duplicates : I.InterlockingTable ! I.InterlockingTable
rename_duplicates(rt) ⌘
if rt = [] then [] else
let
rid = hd rt,
rtTail = rt \ {rid},
−−Check if rid maps to multiple routes
ridSet = { r | r : I.Route • r 2 rng rt ^ r = rt(rid) },
ridSetRemoved = ridSet \ {hd ridSet}
in
if ridSetRemoved = {} then
[ rid 7! rt(rid)] † rename_duplicates(rtTail)
else
separate_duplicates([] , rid , ridSet,"a") † rename_duplicates(
,! rtTail)
end
end
end,
−−Count the ids of all routes which share the same id
separate_duplicates : I.InterlockingTable ⇥T.RouteId ⇥I.Route-set ⇥Text ! I
,! .InterlockingTable
separate_duplicates(rt, rid , ridSet, uniqueString) ⌘
if ridSet = {} then rt else
let
r = hd ridSet,
rName = rid b uniqueString,
newRt = rt † [rName 7! r]
in
separate_duplicates(newRt, rid, ridSet \ {r}, uniqueString b "a")
end
end

TEST_CASE("Protected￿projector￿functions", "[project]"){

,! /mini-withRoutes.xml";

decomposer ! set_req_spec(reqSpecDec);

,! , cc,rt0);

SECTION("Make￿subroutes") {

map<string,RttTgenDkIxlMarkerBoard*> mbs2 = n2 ! getMarkerBoards();
// Convert routetable to a multimap
map<string,RttTgenDkIxlRoute*> rt0Map
= rt0 ! getRoutes();
multimap<string,RttTgenDkIxlRoute*> rt0MultiMap;
for (const auto& r : rt0Map) {
rt0MultiMap.emplace(r.first,r.second);
}
==PAGE:161==
C.2 C++ tests
147
multimap<string,RttTgenDkIxlRoute*> rt1;
multimap<string,RttTgenDkIxlRoute*> rt2;
multimap<string,RttTgenDkIxlRoute*> rt0Rest;
for (const auto& r : rt0MultiMap) {
string rSrc = r.second ! getSourceOid();
string rDest = r.second ! getDestinationOid();
vector<string> rPath = r.second ! getTvdOids();
bool pathInN1 = true;
bool pathInN2 = true;
for (const auto& s : rPath) {
if (!decAUX ! s_exists(s,n1)) {
pathInN1 = false;
}
if (!decAUX ! s_exists(s,n2)) {
pathInN2 = false;
}
if (!pathInN1 && !pathInN2) {
break;
}
}
// Routes which are wholly in n1
if (mbs1.count(rSrc) == 1 && mbs1.count(rDest) == 1 && pathInN1) {
rt1.emplace(r.first,r.second);
} else if (mbs2.count(rSrc) == 1 && mbs2.count(rDest) == 1 &&
,! pathInN2) {
// Routes which are wholly in n2
rt2.emplace(r.first,r.second);
} else {
// Routes which must be turned into subroutes
rt0Rest.emplace(r.first,r.second);
}
}
projector ! WRAPmake_subroutes(rt0Rest,&rt1,&rt2,n1,n2, n0, cc);
REQUIRE(rt1.size() == 8);
REQUIRE(rt2.size() == 8);
}
